011                                                A BSTRACT
012
013            In reinforcement learning, specifying reward functions that capture the intended
014            task can be very challenging. Reward learning aims to address this issue by learning
015
               the reward function. However, a learned reward model may have a low error on
               the data distribution, and yet subsequently produce a policy with large regret. We
016
               say that such a reward model has an error-regret mismatch. The main source of
017
               an error-regret mismatch is the distributional shift that commonly occurs during
018            policy optimization. In this paper, we mathematically show that a sufficiently low
019            expected test error of the reward model guarantees low worst-case regret, but that
020            for any fixed expected test error, there exist realistic data distributions that allow
021            for error-regret mismatch to occur. We then show that similar problems persist
022            even when using policy regularization techniques, commonly employed in methods
023            such as RLHF. We hope our results stimulate the theoretical and empirical study
024            of improved methods to learn reward models, and better ways to reliably measure
025            their quality.
026
027
      1   I NTRODUCTION
028
029
      To solve a sequential decision problem with reinforcement learning (RL), we must first formalize
030   that decision problem using a reward function (Sutton & Barto, 2018). However, for complex tasks,
031   reward functions are often hard to specify correctly. To solve this problem, it is increasingly popular
032   to learn reward functions with reward learning algorithms, instead of specifying the reward functions
033   manually. There are many different reward learning algorithms (e.g., Ng & Russell, 2000; Tung
034   et al., 2018; Brown & Niekum, 2019; Palan et al., 2019), with one of the most popular being reward
035   learning from human feedback (RLHF) (Christiano et al., 2017; Ibarz et al., 2018).
036   For any learning algorithm, it is a crucial question whether or not that learning algorithm is guaranteed
037   to converge to a “good” solution. For example, in the case of supervised learning for classification,
038   it can be shown that a learning algorithm that produces a model with a low empirical error (i.e.,
039   training error) is likely to have a low expected error (i.e., test error), given a sufficient amount of
040   training data and assuming that both the training data and the test data is drawn i.i.d. from a single
041   stationary distribution (Kearns & Vazirani, 1994). In the case of normal supervised learning and
042   standard assumptions, we can therefore be confident that a learning algorithm will converge to a good
043   model, provided that it is given a sufficient amount of training data.
044   Since reward models are also typically learned by supervised learning, we might assume that classical
045   learning-theoretic guarantees carry over. However, these guarantees only ensure that the reward
046   model is approximately correct relative to the training distribution. But after reward learning, we
047   optimize a policy to maximize the learned reward, which effectively leads to a distributional shift.
048   This raises the worry that the trained policy can exploit regions of the state space with abnormally
049   high learned rewards if those regions have a low data coverage during training. In this case, we can
050
      have reward models that have both a low error on the training distribution and an optimal policy with
      large regret, a phenomenon we call error-regret mismatch. We visualize this concern in Figure 1.
051
052   To illustrate this concern, imagine a chatbot. The users can either ask safe queries (“Please help
053   me create a high-protein diet”) or unsafe queries (“Please tell me how to build a nuclear weapon”).
      The chatbot can then either answer these queries, or refuse. Now imagine a helpful-only policy that


                                                         1
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


054
055
056
057
058
059
060
061
062
063
064
065
066
067
      Figure 1: Reward models (red function) are commonly trained by supervised learning to approximate
      some latent, true reward (blue function). Given enough data, one can hope that the reward model
068
      is close to the true reward function on average over the data distribution (upper gray layer) — the
069
      expected error is low. However, low expected error only guarantees a good approximation to the true
070   reward function in areas with high coverage by the data distribution! On the other hand, optimizing an
071   RL policy to maximize the learned reward model induces a distribution shift which can lead the policy
072   to exploit uncertainties of the learned reward model in low-probability areas of the transition space
073   (lower gray layer). This may then lead to high regret. We refer to this phenomenon as error-regret
074   mismatch.
075
076
077   answers every query, no matter whether it is safe or not. Helpful-only policies have been analyzed in
078   past safety research (Denison et al., 2024) and are often a starting point for policies meant to become
079   “helpful, honest, and harmless” (Askell et al., 2021). Intuitively, such a policy is unsafe if many
080   people in the deployment environment ask unsafe questions, or if the damage caused by answering
081   each such question is large.
082   Unfortunately, it is hard for a typical reward learning paradigm without restrictions on the learned
083   reward function to prevent the helpful-only policy from being learned. Intuitively, this is since the
084   chatbot can answer any unsafe query in numerous different styles, such that at least one such answer
085   must have a very low probability in the training distribution for the reward model; the reward model
086   can then inflate this answer’s value while achieving a low training error, thus making a helpful-only
087   policy possible. We illustrate this concern in detail in Appendix B.4.
088   To single out the issue of error-regret mismatch in our theoretical analysis, we take the goals of
089   classical learning theory as a given and show that they are not enough to ensure low regret. More
090   precisely, in probably approximately correct (PAC) learning (Kearns & Vazirani, 1994) the goal is to
091   derive a sample size that guarantees a certain likelihood (“P”) of an approximately correct (“AC”)
092   model on new data points sampled from the training distribution. In our results, we assume that we
093   already have an approximately correct reward model on a data distribution, and then investigate what
094
      we can or can not conclude about the regret of policies trained to maximize the modeled reward.
095   Our theoretical analysis shows that guarantees in policy regret are very sensitive to the data distribution
096   used to train the reward model, leading to our notions of safe and unsafe data distributions. Moreover,
097   we find evidence that some MDPs are in a certain sense “too large” to allow for safe data distributions
098   relative to a reasonable reward model error and desired regret bound. We establish for general MDPs:
099         1. As the error of a learned reward model on a data distribution goes to zero, the worst-case re-
100            gret of optimizing a policy according to that reward model also goes to zero (Propositions 3.1
101            and 3.2)
102
            2. However, for any ϵ > 0, whenever a data distribution has sufficiently low coverage of
103
               some bad policy, it is unsafe; in other words, there exists a reward model that achieves
104
               an expected error of ϵ but has a high-regret optimal policy (Proposition 3.3), a case of
105            error-regret mismatch.
106
107         3. As a consequence, when an MDP has a large number of independent bad policies, every
               data distribution is unsafe (Corollary 3.4).


                                                          2
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


108
            4. More precisely, we derive a set of linear constraints that precisely characterize the safe data
109            distributions for a given MDP (Theorem 3.5).
110
111
      We then investigate the case of regularized policy optimization (including KL-regularized policy
112
      optimization, which is commonly used in methods such as RLHF). We derive regularized versions
113   of Propositions 3.1 and 3.3 in Proposition 4.1 and Theorem 4.2. This shows that regularization alone
114   is no principled solution to error-regret mismatch.
115
116
      We then develop several generalizations of our results for different types of data sources for reward
      model training, such as preferences over trajectories (Propositions C.25 and C.26), and trajectory
117
      scoring (Proposition C.24). Lastly, motivated by the recent success of large language models (OpenAI,
118
      2022; Gemini Team, 2023; Anthropic, 2023), we provide an analysis for the special case of RLHF
119   in the contextual bandit case where we prove a stronger version (Theorem 6.1) of the failure mode
120   already discussed in Theorem 4.2 for general MDPs.
121
122
123   1.1   R ELATED WORK
124
125
      Note: We provide a more extensive related work section in Appendix A
126
127   In offline reinforcement learning, we aim to learn low-regret policies for an MDP ⟨S, A, τ, µ0 , R, γ⟩
128   where the reward function (and sometimes transition distribution (Wang et al., 2022b; Uehara & Sun,
129
      2021)) is unknown and must be learned from an offline dataset {(s, a, r)i }ni=1 sampled from a data
      distribution D ∈ ∆(S×A). A key research question is understanding what data coverage conditions
130
      ensure learning a near-optimal policy with an efficient sample complexity. Existing theoretical work
131
      primarily falls into two categories, covering both MDPs (Foster et al., 2021; Wang et al., 2022b;
132   2020; Amortila et al., 2020; Uehara & Sun, 2021; Uehara et al., 2021) and contextual bandits (Nika
133   et al., 2024; Cen et al., 2024):
134
135
      Lower bound results prove that various data-coverage conditions are insufficient for sample-efficient
      offline RL by establishing worst-case sample complexity bounds. Research in this area (Foster et al.,
136
      2021; Wang et al., 2022b; 2020; Amortila et al., 2020; Nika et al., 2024) identifies adversarial MDPs
137
      that satisfy specific data-coverage conditions where achieving low regret is either computationally
138   intractable due to excessive sample requirements (Foster et al., 2021; Wang et al., 2022b; 2020; Nika
139   et al., 2024) or fundamentally impossible regardless of sample size (Amortila et al., 2020).
140
141
      Upper bound results, on the other hand, establish positive guarantees under specific structural
      assumptions. Works in this category (Wang et al., 2022b; 2020; Uehara & Sun, 2021; Nika et al.,
142
      2024; Cen et al., 2024; Song et al., 2024) develop algorithms with provable sample-efficiency bounds
143
      by making structural assumptions about the MDP structure, reward learning process, or policy
144   optimization approach.
145
146   Intuitively, the quality of a reward model that is being approximated from a finite dataset is influenced
147
      by two key factors: the dataset size n and the dataset quality, specifically how well the data distribution
      D covers the data space S×A. Prior work confirms this intuition, with most works deriving
148
      variants of the following     template (see for example recent work Nika et al. (2024)): Regret ∈
149
      O poly Cov·Struct

                      n         . Here, Cov represents some measure of the coverage of D, while Struct
150   captures the structural assumptions of the specific approach. Such structural assumptions may
151   include: realizability of function classes (Wang et al., 2022b; Uehara & Sun, 2021; Foster et al., 2021;
152   Nika et al., 2024), linear function approximation (Nika et al., 2024; Cen et al., 2024; Wang et al.,
153   2022b), and various constraints on reward- or policy functions (Wang et al., 2020; Uehara & Sun,
154   2021; Nika et al., 2024).
155
      Our paper differs from these works in two key aspects: a) we explicitly analyze how the reward
156   modeling error ϵ affects the final policy regret, rather than focusing on the number of samples (prior
157   works only implicitly consider ϵ), and b) we examine worst-case scenarios instead of probabilistic
158   guarantees. The most relevant work in this area is Song et al. (2024), which analyzes RLHF
159   specifically. Their setup in section 3, combined with their Assumption 3, perfectly recovers our safe
160   distribution definition (see Definition 2.1) when applied to the special case of RLHF and √
                                                                                                when
                                                                                                     using
161   the mean squared error metric. Their Theorem 4.2 demonstrates that Regret ∈ O Cov · ϵ , where
      the square root emerges from using the mean squared error during the reward learning step.


                                                          3
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


162
      While Song et al. (2024) focus on RLHF with mean-squared error metric, we provide similar
163   results for general classes of regularized and unregularized policy optimization (for both MDPs
164   and contextual bandits), as well as a wide range of different error metrics. Similar to prior sample-
165   complexity results, we investigate the influence of different coverage constraints on regret guarantees.
166   For our initial results (Propositions 3.1, 3.2 and 4.1) we use the condition min(s,a) D(s, a) > 0. Since
167   we assume that all states of our MDPs are reachable, this is equivalent to a full coverage condition
168   (see Table 1 of Uehara & Sun (2021) for an overview of different coverage conditions). We then
169   relax the constraints to partial coverage constraints and prove several negative results (Proposition 3.3
170   and theorems 4.2 and 6.1). Finally, we fully generalize our results from Propositions 3.1 to 3.3
171
      and corollary 3.4 into a single theorem (Theorem 3.5) which allows us to determine the worst-case
      safety of arbitrary data distributions. To the best of our knowledge, we are the first work to achieve
172
      such a level of generality.
173
174   2     P RELIMINARIES
175
176   A Markov Decision Process (MDP) is a tuple ⟨S, A, τ, µ0 , R, γ⟩ where S is a set of states, A is a
177   set of actions, τ : S×A → ∆(S) is a transition function, µ0 ∈ ∆(S) is an initial state distribution,
178   R : S×A → R is a reward function, and γ ∈ (0, 1) is a discount rate. We define the range of a
179   reward function R as range R := max(s,a)∈S×A R(s, a) − min(s,a)∈S×A R(s, a).
180
      A policy is a function π : S → ∆(A). We denote the set of all policies by Π. A trajectory
181   ξ = ⟨s0 , a0 , s1 , a1 , ...⟩ is a possible path P
                                                       in an MDP. The return function G gives the cumulative
                                                         ∞
182   discounted reward of a trajectory, G(ξ) = t=0 γ t R(st , at ), and the evaluation function J gives the
183   expected trajectory return given a policy, J(π) = Eξ∼π [G(ξ)]. A policy maximizing J is an optimal
184   policy. We define the regret of a policy π with respect to reward function R as
185
                                            maxπ′ ∈Π JR (π ′ ) − JR (π)
186                       RegR (π) :=                                            ∈ [0, 1].
187
                                         maxπ′ ∈Π JR (π ′ ) − minπ′ ∈Π JR (π ′ )
188   Here, JR is the policy evaluation function for R.
189
      In this paper, we assume that S and A are finite, and that all states are reachable under τ and µ0 . We
190
      also assume that max JR − min JR ̸= 0 (since the reward function would otherwise be trivial). Note
191
      that this implies that range R > 0, and that RegR (π) is well-defined.
192
193   The state-action occupancy measure is a function η : Π → R|S×A| mapping each policy π ∈ Π
194   to the corresponding "state-action occupancy measure", describing the discounted   P∞frequency that
195   each state-action tuple is visited by a policy. Formally, η(π)(s, a) = η π (s, a) = t=0 γ t · P (st =
196   s, at = a | ξ ∼ π). Note that by writing the reward function R as a vector R     ⃗ ∈ R|S×A| , we can
                                                           π ⃗
197   split J into a function that is linear in R: J(π) = η · R. By normalizing a state-action occupancy
198   measure η π we obtain a policy-induced distribution Dπ := (1 − γ) · η π .
199
      2.1   P ROBLEM FORMALIZATION OF RL WITH REWARD LEARNING
200
201   In RL with reward learning, we assume that we have an MDP ⟨S, A, τ, µ0 , R, γ⟩ where the reward
202   function R is unknown. We may also assume that τ and µ0 are unknown, as long as we can sample
203   from them (though S, A, and γ must generally be known, at least implicitly). We then first learn a
204   reward model R̂ that approximates the true reward R and then optimize a policy π̂ to maximize R̂.
205   The aim of this two-step procedure is for π̂ to achieve low regret under the true reward function R.
206   We now formalize these aspects in detail for our theoretical analysis, with a visualization provided in
207   Figure 2:
208   Reward learning We first learn a reward model R̂ from data. There are many possible data sources
209   for reward learning, like demonstrations (Ng & Russell, 2000), preferences over trajectories (Chris-
210   tiano et al., 2017), or even the initial environment state (Shah et al., 2019); a taxonomy can be found
211   in (Jeon et al., 2020). Since we are concerned with problems that remain even when the reward
212   model is already approximately correct, we abstract away the data sources and training procedures
213   and assume that we learn a reward model R̂ which satisfies
214                                                "                      #
                                                     |R̂(s, a) − R(s, a)|
215                                     E(s,a)∼D                            ≤ ϵ                            (1)
                                                           range R

      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025

                                                  Learning                           Optimization

                                                        Guaranteed by Safe Data Distributions
224
225
      Figure 2: An abstract model of the classical reward learning pipeline. A reward model R̂ is trained
226   to approximate the true reward function R under some data distribution D. The training process
227
      converges when R̂ is similar to R in expectation (see 1 ). In the second step, a policy π̂ is trained to
228   achieve high learned reward, possibly involving a regularization (see 2 ). We are interested in the
229   question of when exactly this training process guarantees that π̂ has low regret. More formally, we
230   call a data distribution D safe whenever the implication 1 =⇒ 3 holds for all reward models R̂
231   that satisfy 1 .
232
233
234   for some ϵ > 0 and stationary distribution D over transitions S×A. Note that this is the true
235   expectation under D, rather than an estimate of this expectation based on some finite sample. We
236
      divide by range R, since the absolute error ϵ is only meaningful relative to the overall scale of the
      reward R.
237
238   To be clear, most reward learning algorithms cannot guarantee a bound as in Equation (1) since most
239   realistic data sources do not determine the true reward function, even for infinite data (Skalse et al.,
240   2023). Instead, we choose Equation (1) because it serves as an upper bound to many common reward
241   learning training objectives (see Appendix C.5). Thus, when we show in later sections that high regret
242
      is possible even when this inequality holds, then this problem can be expected to generalize to other
      data sources. We make this generalization precise for some data sources in Section 5. In particular,
243
      we will show that Equation (1) implies a low cross-entropy error between the choice distributions of
244
      the true reward function and the reward model, as is commonly used for RLHF, e.g. in the context of
245   language models (Ziegler et al., 2019).
246
247
      Policy optimization Given R̂, we then learn a policy π̂ by solving the MDP ⟨S, A, τ, µ0 , R̂, γ⟩.
248   In the most straightforward case, we do this by simply finding a policy that is optimal according
249   to R̂. However, it is also common to perform regularized optimization. In that case, we make use
250
      of an additional regularization function ω : Π → R, with ω(π) ≥ 0 for all π ∈ Π. Given R̂, a
251   regularization function ω, and a regularization weight λ ∈ [0, ∞), we say that π̂ is (λ, ω)-optimal if
252
253
                                                π̂ ∈ arg max JR̂ (π) − λω(π).                                    (2)
                                                             π
254
      Typically, λ punishes large deviations from some reference policy πref , e.g. with the regularization
255   function given by the KL-divergence ω(π) = DKL (π||πref ). πref may also be used to collect training
256   data for the reward learning algorithm, in which case we may assume D = Dπ in Equation (1). Most
257   of our results to not depend on these specific instantiations, however.
258
259   Regret minimization The aim of the previous two steps is for the policy π̂ to have low regret
260   RegR (π̂) under the true reward function R. Our question is thus if and when it is sufficient to ensure
261   that R̂ satisfies Equation 1, in order to guarantee that a policy π̂ optimal according to Equation (2)
262   has low regret RegR (π̂).
263
264   2.2   S AFE DATA DISTRIBUTIONS
265
266   We now make the elaborations from the previous subsections more concrete by providing a formal
267   definition of a safe data distribution. In particular, we say that a data distribution D is safe, whenever
268   it holds that for every reward model R̂ that satisfies Equation (1) for D, all optimal policies of R̂
269   have low regret. We provide a visualization of this concept in Figure 2 and a formal definition in
      Definition 2.1.


                                                                        5
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


270
      Definition 2.1 (Safe- and unsafe data distributions). For a given MDP ⟨S, A, τ, µ0 , R, γ⟩, let ϵ > 0,
271   L ∈ [0, 1], and λ ∈ [0, ∞). Let ω be a continuous function with ω(π) ≥ 0 for all π ∈ Π. Then the
272   set of safe data distributions safe(R, ϵ, L, λ, ω) is the set of all distributions D ∈ ∆(S×A) such that
273   for all possible reward models R̂ : S×A → R and policies π̂ : S → ∆(A) that satisfy the following
274   two properties:
275                                                                                   h            i
276          1. Low expected error: R̂ is ϵ-close to R under D, i.e., E(s,a)∼D |R̂(s,a)−R(s,a)|
                                                                                           range R   ≤ ϵ.
277
278         2. Optimality: π̂ is (λ, ω)-optimal with respect to R̂, i.e. π̂ ∈ arg maxπ JR̂ (π) − λω(π).
279
280   we can guarantee that π̂ has regret smaller than L, i.e.:
281
282         3. Low regret: π̂ has a regret smaller than L with respect to R, i.e., RegR (π̂) < L.
283
      Similarly, we define the set of unsafe data distributions to be the complement of safe(R, ϵ, L, λ, ω):
284
285                unsafe(R, ϵ, L, λ, ω)      :=    { D ∈ ∆(S×A) | D ∈
                                                                     / safe(R, ϵ, L, λ, ω)}.
286
287
      Thus, unsafe(R, ϵ, L, λ, ω) consists of the data distributions D for which there exists a reward model
288   R̂ that is ϵ-close to R and a policy π̂ that is (λ, π)-optimal with respect to R̂, but such that π̂ has large
289   regret RegR (π̂) ≥ L. In this sense, we are operating under a worst-case framework for the reward
290
      model and policy learned by our training algorithms. Whenever we consider the unregularized
      case (λ = 0 or ω = 0), we drop the λ and ω to ease the notation and just use safe(R, ϵ, L) and
291
      unsafe(R, ϵ, L) instead. Lastly, we mention that while we use the mean absolute error (MAE) in
292
      condition 1, one could in principle also work with the mean-squared error. All our results then have
293   analogous versions. We explain this in Appendix B.3.
294
295   Note: Throughout this paper, we will use the terminology that a data distribution D “allows for
296
      error-regret mismatch” as a colloquial term to express that D ∈ unsafe(R, ϵ, L, λ, ω).
297
298   3    E RROR - REGRET MISMATCH FOR UNREGULARIZED POLICY OPTIMIZATION
299
300   In this section, we investigate the case where no regularization is used in the policy optimization stage.
301   We seek to determine if it is sufficient for a reward model to be close to the true reward function on a
302   data distribution in order to ensure low regret for the learned policy.
303   In our first result, we show that under certain conditions, a low expected error ϵ does indeed guarantee
304   that policy optimization will yield a policy with low regret.
305
      Proposition 3.1. Let ⟨S, A, τ, µ0 , R, γ⟩ be an arbitrary MDP, let L ∈ (0, 1], and let D ∈ ∆(S×A)
306
      be a positive data distribution (i.e., a distribution such that D(s, a) > 0 for all (s, a) ∈ S×A). Then
307
      there exists an ϵ > 0 such that D ∈ safe(R, ϵ, L).
308
309   The proof of Proposition 3.1 can be found in Appendix D.1 (see Corollary D.7) and is based on an
310   application of Berge’s maximum theorem (Berge, 1963), and the fact that the expected distance
311   between the true reward function and the learned reward model under D is induced from a norm. See
312   Theorem 6.1 for a similar result in which the expected error in rewards is replaced by an expected
313   error in choice probabilities.
314   One might be inclined to conclude that the guarantee of Proposition 3.1 allows one to practically
315   achieve low regret by ensuring a low error ϵ (as measured by Equation 1). However, in the following
316   result we provide a more detailed analysis that shows that low regret requires a prohibitively low ϵ:
317   Proposition 3.2. Let the setting be as in Proposition 3.1. If ϵ > 0 satisfies
318
                                     1 − γ range J R
319                                ϵ< √ ·            · min D(s, a) · L
320                                     2   range R (s,a)∈S×A
321   then D ∈ safe(R, ϵ, L).
322
323   The proof can be found in Theorem
                                     √     D.11, Appendix D.2. Example D.13 shows that the bound
      on ϵ is tight up to a factor of 2. This result is problematic in practice due to the dependence on


                                                           6
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


324
      the minimum of D. Realistic MDPs usually contain a massive amount of states and actions, which
325   necessarily requires D to give a very small support to at least some transitions. The dependence of
326   the upper bound on D also shows that there is no ϵ for which every distribution D is guaranteed to be
327   safe, as min(s,a)∈D D(s, a) can be arbitrarily small. We concretize this intuition by showing that in
328   every MDP and for every ϵ > 0, there exist weak assumptions for which a data distribution allows
329   for a large error-regret mismatch.
330   Proposition 3.3. Let M = ⟨S, A, τ, µ0 , R, γ⟩ be an MDP, D ∈ ∆(S×A) a data distribution,
331   ϵ > 0, and L ∈ [0, 1]. Assume there exists a policy π̂ with the property that RegR (π̂) ≥ L and
332   D(supp Dπ̂ ) < ϵ, where supp Dπ̂ is defined as the set of state-action pairs (s, a) ∈ S×A such that
333   Dπ̂ (s, a) > 0. In other words, there is a “bad” policy for R that is not very supported by D. Then,
334   D allows for error-regret mismatch to occur, i.e., D ∈ unsafe(R, ϵ, L).
335
336   The proof of Proposition 3.3 can be found in Appendix C.2 (see Proposition C.5). The intuition is
337   straightforward: There exists a reward model R̂ that is very similar to the true reward function R
338   outside the support of Dπ̂ but has very large rewards for the support of Dπ̂ . Because D(supp Dπ̂ ) is
339   very small, this still allows R̂ to have a very small expected error w.r.t. to D, while π̂, the optimal
340   policy for R̂, will have regret at least L. To avoid confusions, we show in Proposition C.7 that the
341   assumptions on ϵ in Proposition 3.2 and Proposition 3.3 cannot hold simultaneously. This is as
342   expected since otherwise the conclusions of these propositions would imply that a data distribution
343   can be both safe and unsafe.
344   Note that the conditions for unsafe data distributions in Proposition 3.3 also cover positive data
345   distributions (that we showed to be eventually safe for small enough ϵ in Proposition 3.1). Furthermore,
346   especially in very large MDPs, it is very likely that the data distribution will not sufficiently cover
347   large parts of the support of some policies, especially since the number of (deterministic) policies
348   grows exponentially with the number of states. Sometimes, this can lead to all data distributions
349   being unsafe, as we show in the following corollary:
350   Corollary 3.4. Let M = ⟨S, A, τ, µ0 , R, γ⟩ be an MDP, ϵ > 0, and L ∈ [0, 1]. Assume there exists
351   a set of policies ΠL with:
352
353          • RegR (π) ≥ L for all π ∈ ΠL ;
354                                   ′
             • supp Dπ ∩ supp Dπ = ∅ for all π, π ′ ∈ ΠL ; and
355
356          • |ΠL | ≥ 1/ϵ.
357
358   Then unsafe(R, ϵ, L) = ∆(S × A), i.e.: all distributions are unsafe.
359
360
      The proof of Corollary 3.4 can be found in Appendix C.2 (see Corollary C.6).
361   Corollary 3.4 outlines sufficient conditions for a scenario where all possible data distributions are
362   unsafe for a given MDP. This happens when there exist many different policies with large regret and
363   disjoint support, which requires there to be a large action space. This could for example happen in
364   the case of a language model interacting with a user if there are many mutually distinct styles to
365   answer unsafe queries. We illustrated this concern in slightly more detail in the introduction, and in
366
      full detail in Appendix B.4.
367   We conclude by stating the main result of this section, which unifies all previous results and derives the
368   most general conditions, i.e. necessary and sufficient conditions, for when exactly a data distribution
369   allows for error-regret mismatch to occur:
370   Theorem 3.5. For all MDPs ⟨S, A, τ, µ0 , R, γ⟩ and L ∈ [0, 1], there exists a matrix M such that for
371   all ϵ > 0 and D ∈ ∆(S×A) we have:
372
373
                              D ∈ safe(R, ϵ, L)      ⇐⇒       M · D > ϵ · range R · 1,                       (3)
374   where we use the vector notation of D, and 1 is a vector containing all ones.
375
376   The proof of Theorem 3.5 can be found in Appendix C.3 (see Theorem C.16) and largely relies on
377   geometric arguments that arise from comparing the set of unsafe reward models and the set of reward
      models that are close to the true reward function. Interestingly, this means that the set of safe data


                                                          7
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


378
      distributions resembles a polytope, in the sense that it is a convex set and is defined by the intersection
379   of an open polyhedral set (defined by the system of strict inequalities M · D > ϵ · range R · 1), and
380   the closed data distribution simplex.
381
382
      While Theorem 3.5 only proves the existence of such a matrix M , we provide further results and
      analyses in the appendix, namely:
383
384             1. In Appendix C.3.2 we derive closed-form expressions of the rows of matrix M , and show
385                that its entries depend on multiple factors, such as the original reward function R, the state
386                transition distribution τ , and the set of deterministic policies that achieve regret at least L.
387
                2. In Appendix C.3.3 we provide an algorithm to compute matrix M .
388
                3. In Appendix C.3.4 we provide a worked example of computing and visualizing the set of
389
                   safe distributions for a toy example.
390
391   Lastly, we note that M does not depend on ϵ, and M only contains non-negative entries (see
392   Appendix C.3.2). This allows us to recover Proposition 3.1, since by letting ϵ approach zero, the set
393   of data distributions that fulfill the conditions in Equation (3) approaches the entire data distribution
394   simplex. On the other hand, the dependence of M on the true reward function and the underlying
395   MDP implies that computing M is infeasible in practice since many of these components are not
396   known, restricting the use of M to theoretical analysis.
397
398   4       E RROR - REGRET MISMATCH FOR REGULARIZED POLICY OPTIMIZATION
399
400   In this section, we investigate the error-regret mismatch for regularized policy optimization. First, we
401   prove that for almost any reference policy πref that achieves regret L and minimizes the regularization
402   term ω, there exists a sufficiently small ϵ such that reward learning within ϵ of the true reward function
403   preserves the regret bound L.
404   Proposition 4.1. Let λ ∈ (0, ∞), let ⟨S, A, τ, µ0 , R, γ⟩ be any MDP, and let D ∈ S×A be any
405   data distribution that assigns positive probability to all transitions. Let ω : Π → R be a continuous
406   regularization function that has a reference policy πref as a minimum.1 Assume that πref is not (λ, ω)-
407   optimal for R and let L = RegR (πref ). Then there exists ϵ > 0 such that D ∈ safe(R, ϵ, L, λ, ω).
408
409   The proof of Proposition 4.1 can be found in Appendix D.4 (see Theorem D.21) and is again an
410   application of Berge’s theorem (Berge, 1963). Note that the regret bound L is defined as the regret of
411   the reference policy. This makes intuitively sense, as regularized policy optimization constrains the
412   policy under optimization π̂ to not deviate too strongly from the reference policy πref , which will also
413
      constrain the regret of π̂ to stay close to the regret of πref . Under the conditions of Proposition 4.1, the
414
      regret of πref serves as an upper regret bound because for small enough ϵ the learned reward R̂ and
415   the true reward R are close enough such that maximizing R̂ also improve reward with respect to R.
416
      Furthermore, we note that it is also possible to derive a version of the theorem in which the expected
      error in rewards is replaced by a KL divergence in choice probabilities, similar to Proposition D.14,
417
      by combining the arguments in that proposition with the arguments in Berge’s theorem. A full
418
      formulation and proof of the result can be found in Theorem D.22.
419
420   Similar to Proposition 3.1, Proposition 4.1 does not guarantee the existence of a universal ϵ such that
421   all data distributions D are in safe(R, ϵ, L, λ, ω). In our next result, we show that such an ϵ does
422
      not exist, since for each ϵ, there is a nontrivial set of data distributions that allows for error-regret
      mismatch to occur:
423
424   Theorem 4.2. Let M = ⟨S, A, τ, µ0 , R, γ⟩ be an arbitrary MDP, λ ∈ (0, ∞), L ∈ (0, 1), and
425   ω : Π → R be a regularization function. Furthermore, let π∗ be a determinstic worst-case policy
426   for R, meaning that RegR (π∗ ) = 1. Let C := C(M, π∗ , L, λ, ω) < ∞ be the constant defined in
427
      Equation (107) in the appendix. Let ϵ > 0. Then for all data distributions D ∈ ∆(S×A) with
428                                                                ϵ
                                              D(supp Dπ∗ ) ≤            ,                                   (4)
429                                                              1+C
430   we have D ∈ unsafe(R, ϵ, L, λ, ω).
431
          1
              E.g., if πref (a | s) > 0 for all (s, a) ∈ S×A and ω(π) := DKL (π||πref ), then the minimum is πref .


                                                                8
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


432
      The proof of Theorem 4.2 can be found in Appendix C.5 (see Theorem C.41). The general idea is as
433
      follows: To prove that D is unsafe, define R̂ to be equal to R outside of supp Dπ∗ , and very large in
434
      supp Dπ∗ . If it is sufficiently large in this region, then regularized optimization leads to a policy π̂
435
      with RegR (π̂) ≥ L. Finally, the condition that D(supp Dπ∗ ) ≤ 1+C      ϵ
                                                                                  ensures that R̂ has a reward
436
      error bounded by ϵ.
437
438   Note that Theorem 4.2 is very general and covers a large class of different regularization methods. In
439   Corollary C.43 we provide a specialized result for the case of KL-regularized policy optimization,
440
      and in Section 6 we investigate error-regret mismatch in the RLHF framework. At the end of our
      conceptual example described in the introduction and in detail in Appendix B.4, we also discuss the
441
      simple intuition that simply giving a low enough training probability to some unsafe actions can be
442
      enough to lead to unsafe reward inference and policy optimization even in the regularized case. This
443   is in accordance with Theorem 4.2.
444
445
446   5    G ENERALIZATION OF THE ERROR MEASUREMENT
447
448   Our results have so far expressed the error of the learned reward R̂ in terms of Equation (1), i.e.,
449   in terms of the expected error of individual transitions. In Appendix C.4.1, we show that many
450   common reward learning training objectives can be upper-bounded in terms of the expected error
451   metric defined in Equation (1). This in turn means that our negative results generalize to reward
452
      learning algorithms that use these other training objectives. In particular, if we have two error metrics
453   f (R, R̂), g(R, R̂), such that for all R, R̂ we have g(R, R̂) < f (R, R̂), then it holds for any arbitrary
454
      data distribution D ∈ S×A that:
455                    D ∈ unsafef (R, ϵ, L, λ, ω)      =⇒      D ∈ unsafeg (R, ϵ, L, λ, ω)
456
457
458
      6    E RROR - REGRET MISMATCH IN RLHF
459
460   In this section we use the generalization results from Section 5 to extend our results to reinforcement
461
      learning from human feedback (RLHF). We provide more general results about the class of KL-
      regularized optimization policy optimization methods in Appendix C.4.6.
462
463   RLHF, especially in the context of large language models, is usually modeled in a contextual bandit
464   setting (Ziegler et al., 2019; Stiennon et al., 2020; Bai et al., 2022; Ouyang et al., 2022; Rafailov
465   et al., 2023). A contextual bandit ⟨S, A, µ0 , R⟩ is defined by a set of states S, a set of actions A, a
466   data distribution µ0 ∈ ∆(S), and a reward function R : S×A → R. The goal is to learn a policy
467
      π : S → ∆(A) that maximizes the expected return J(π) = Es∼µ0 ,a∼π(·|s) [R(s, a)]. In the context
      of language models, S is usually called the set of prompts or contexts, and A the set of responses.
468
469   We state the following theorem using a more precise version of Definition 2.1 tailored to the
470   RLHF setting. In particular, we replace the similarity metric (property 1. of Definition 2.1)
471   with the expected similarity in choice probabilities. A precise mathematical definition can be
472   found in Appendix C.4.4. We denote   the resulting sets of safe- and unsafe data
                                                                                     distributions by
473   safeRLHF R, ϵ, L, λ, DKL (·||πref ) and unsafeRLHF R, ϵ, L, λ, DKL (·||πref ) .
474   By making use of the specifics of this setting, we can derive more interpretable and stronger results.
475   In particular, we specify a set of reference distributions for which performing KL-regularized policy
476   optimization allows for error-regret mismatch to occur.
477
      Theorem 6.1. Let ⟨S, A, µ0 , R⟩ be a contextual bandit. Given L ∈ [0, 1), we define for every state
478   s ∈ S the reward threshold:RL (s) := (1 − L) · maxa∈A R(s, a) + L · mina∈A R(s, a). Lastly,
479   let πref : S → A be an arbitrary reference policy for which it holds that for every (s, a) ∈ S×A,
480   πref (a|s) > 0, and there exists at least one action as ∈ A such that R(s, as ) < RL (s) and πref (as |s)
481   satisfies the following inequality:
482
483                                      (RL (s) − R(s, as ))       range R       ϵ2
                        πref (as |s) ≤                        ·      1
                                                                                ·      .
484                                               L             exp λ · range R 4 · λ2
485
      Let Dref (s, a) := µ0 (s) · πref (a|s). Then Dref ∈ unsafeRLHF R, 2 · ϵ, L, λ, DKL (·||πref )



                                                          9
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


486
      The proof of Theorem 6.1 can be found in Appendix C.4.5 (see Propositions C.34 and C.35). We
487   expect the conditions on the reference policy πref to be likely to hold in real-world cases as the
488   number of potential actions (or responses) is usually very large, and language models typically assign
489   a large portion of their probability mass to only a tiny fraction of all responses. This means that for
490   every state/prompt s, a huge majority of actions/responses a have a very small probability πref (a | s).
491   See also our conceptual example in the introduction and Appendix B.4 to make this intuition concrete.
492
493
494
495
      7     D ISCUSSION
496
497   In this paper, we contributed to the foundations of reward learning theory by studying the relationship
498   between the training error of the learned reward function and the regret of policies that then result
499   from policy optimization. We showed that as the expected error of a reward model R̂ goes to zero,
500   the regret of the resulting policy (with or without regularization) also goes to zero (Proposition 3.1)
501   or is bounded by the regret of a reference policy (Proposition 4.1). However, in Proposition 3.2 we
502   showed that the training error needed to ensure a certain regret is proportional to the minimum of
503   the data distribution D. Consequently, there exists no training error that can universally ensure low
504   regret.
505   More specifically, low expected error of R̂ does not ensure low regret for all realistic data distributions
506   (Proposition 3.3, Theorem 4.2 and Theorem 6.1). We refer to this phenomenon as error-regret
507   mismatch. This is due to policy optimization involving a distributional shift. Moreover, for some
508   MDPs with very large action spaces there does not exist any safe data distribution relative to a
509   reasonable reward model error and desired regret bound (Corollary 3.4). We also showed that our
510   results generalize to other data sources, such as preferences over trajectories (Propositions C.25
511   and C.26) and trajectory scores (Proposition C.24), supporting the conclusion that this issue is a
512   fundamental problem of reward learning.
513   Lastly, for the case of unregularized optimization, we derive a set of necessary and sufficient
514   conditions based on linear programming that allow us to determine the set of safe data distributions
515   for arbitrary MDPs, thereby completely answering the question of when exactly a data distribution is
516   safe (Theorem 3.5).
517   7.1   L IMITATIONS AND FUTURE WORK
518
519
      Our work focuses on a worst-case setting with respect to the learned reward function and and optimal
520
      policy. Future work could take the inductive biases of common optimization procedures into account
521
      and consider non-optimal policies that result from realistic training processes. One could also attempt
522   to analyze the likelihood of high-regret instead of simply proving its existence.
523
524   Furthermore, it is important to theoretically analyze improved reward learning and policy optimization
525
      procedures. There is already some empirical work on using reward model ensembles (Coste et al.,
      2023) or weight averaged reward models (Ramé et al., 2024) to overcome problems of reward
526
      model overoptimization. In the special case of multi-armed bandits, iterated data-smoothing has
527
      been proposed and analyzed theoretically and empirically (Zhu et al., 2024). Very recent work also
528   considers learning reward models on online data for mitigating distribution shifts and thus reward
529   overoptimization (Lang et al., 2024a) or even theoretically analyzes such a setting for the special case
530   of linear reward functions (Song et al., 2024). We hope that a careful theoretical analysis of all these
531   settings in similar generality as our work can identify reliable ways to improve upon the “theoretical
532   baseline” established by our work.
533
      In addition to improving the theory and practice of reward learning itself, there are other ways to
534   improve safety. For example, one could research evaluation methods for learned reward functions
535   that go beyond looking at the training error, e.g. by using interpretability methods (Michaud et al.,
536   2020; Jenner & Gleave, 2022) or finding better ways to quantify reward function distance (Gleave
537   et al., 2020; Skalse et al., 2024). We are also excited about efforts to evaluate policies for dangerous
538   capabilities (Phuong et al., 2024), red-teaming (Perez et al., 2022), safety cases (Clymer et al., 2024),
539   shields (Alshiekh et al., 2018), and a numerous suite of other approaches (Anwar et al., 2024). All of
      these are largely unsolved research problems that deserve further attention.

811
      A PPENDIX
812   This appendix develops the theory outlined in the main paper in a self-contained and complete way,
813   including all proofs. In Appendix B, we present the setup of all concepts and the problem formulation,
814   as was already contained in the main paper. In Appendix C, we present all “negative results”.
815   Conditional on an error threshold in the reward model, these results present conditions for the data
816
      distribution that allow reward models to be learned that allow for error-regret mismatch. That section
      also contains Theorem C.16 which is an equivalent condition for the absence of error-regret mismatch
817
      but could be considered a statement about error-regret mismatch by negation. In Appendix D, we
818
      present sufficient conditions for safe optimization in several settings. Typically, this boils down to
819   showing that given a data distribution, a sufficiently small error in the reward model guarantees that
820   its optimal policies have low regret.
821

874
875   A    E XTENDED RELATED WORK
876
877   Reward Learning Reward learning is a key concept in reinforcement learning that involves learning
878   the reward function for complex tasks with latent and difficult-to-specify reward functions. Many
879   methods have been developed to incorporate various types of human feedback into the reward learning
880
      process (Wirth et al., 2017; Ng et al., 2000; Bajcsy et al., 2017; Jeon et al., 2020).
881
882
      Challenges in Reward Learning Reward learning presents several challenges (Casper et al., 2023;
      Lang et al., 2024b; Skalse & Abate, 2023; 2024), such as reward misgeneralization, where the
883
      reward model learns a different reward function that performs well on in-distribution data but differs
884
      strongly on out-of-distribution data (Skalse et al., 2023). This can lead to unintended consequences
885   in real-world applications.
886
887   Reward misgeneralization can also result in reward hacking (Krakovna, 2020), a consequence of
888
      Goodhart’s law (Goodhart, 1984; Zhuang & Hadfield-Menell, 2020; Hennessy & Goodhart, 2023;
      Strathern, 1997; Karwowski et al., 2023). Reward hacking has been extensively studied both
889
      theoretically (Skalse et al., 2022; 2024; Zhuang & Hadfield-Menell, 2020) and empirically (Zhang
890
      et al., 2018; Farebrother et al., 2018; Cobbe et al., 2019; Krakovna, 2020; Gao et al., 2023; Tien et al.,
891   2022).
892
893
      Offline RL In offline reinforcement learning, we aim to learn low-regret policies for an MDP
894   ⟨S, A, τ, µ0 , R, γ⟩ where the reward function (and sometimes transition distribution (Wang et al.,
895   2022b; Uehara & Sun, 2021)) is unknown and must be learned from an offline dataset {(s, a, r)i }ni=1
896   sampled from a data distribution D ∈ ∆(S×A). A key research question is understanding what
897   data coverage conditions ensure learning a near-optimal policy with an efficient sample complexity.
898   Existing theoretical work primarily falls into two categories, covering both MDPs (Foster et al.,
899   2021; Wang et al., 2022b; 2020; Amortila et al., 2020; Uehara & Sun, 2021; Uehara et al., 2021) and
900   contextual bandits (Nika et al., 2024; Cen et al., 2024):
901   Lower bound results prove that various data-coverage conditions are insufficient for sample-efficient
902   offline RL by establishing worst-case sample complexity bounds. Research in this area (Foster et al.,
903   2021; Wang et al., 2022b; 2020; Amortila et al., 2020; Nika et al., 2024) identifies adversarial MDPs
904   that satisfy specific data-coverage conditions where achieving low regret is either computationally
905   intractable due to excessive sample requirements (Foster et al., 2021; Wang et al., 2022b; 2020; Nika
906   et al., 2024) or fundamentally impossible regardless of sample size (Amortila et al., 2020).
907   Upper bound results, on the other hand, establish positive guarantees under specific structural
908   assumptions. Works in this category (Wang et al., 2022b; 2020; Uehara & Sun, 2021; Nika et al.,
909   2024; Cen et al., 2024; Song et al., 2024) develop algorithms with provable sample-efficiency bounds
910   by making structural assumptions about the MDP structure, reward learning process, or policy
911   optimization approach.
912   Intuitively, the quality of a reward model that is being approximated from a finite dataset is influenced
913   by two key factors: the dataset size n and the dataset quality, specifically how well the data distribution
914   D covers the data space S×A. Prior work confirms this intuition, with most works deriving
915   variants of the following     template (see for example recent work Nika et al. (2024)): Regret ∈
      O poly Cov·Struct

916                   n         . Here, Cov represents some measure of the coverage of D, while Struct
917   captures the structural assumptions of the specific approach. Such structural assumptions may
      include: realizability of function classes (Wang et al., 2022b; Uehara & Sun, 2021; Foster et al., 2021;


                                                         17
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


918
      Nika et al., 2024), linear function approximation (Nika et al., 2024; Cen et al., 2024; Wang et al.,
919   2022b), and various constraints on reward- or policy functions (Wang et al., 2020; Uehara & Sun,
920   2021; Nika et al., 2024).
921
922
      Our paper differs from these works in two key aspects: a) we explicitly analyze how the reward
      modeling error ϵ affects the final policy regret, rather than focusing on the number of samples (prior
923
      works only implicitly consider ϵ), and b) we examine worst-case scenarios instead of probabilistic
924
      guarantees. The most relevant work in this area is Song et al. (2024), which analyzes RLHF
925   specifically. Their setup in section 3, combined with their Assumption 3, perfectly recovers our safe
926   distribution definition (see Definition 2.1) when applied to the special case of RLHF and √
                                                                                                when
                                                                                                     using
927   the mean squared error metric. Their Theorem 4.2 demonstrates that Regret ∈ O Cov · ϵ , where
928   the square root emerges from using the mean squared error during the reward learning step.
929
      While Song et al. (2024) focus on RLHF with mean-squared error metric, we provide similar
930
      results for general classes of regularized and unregularized policy optimization (for both MDPs
931   and contextual bandits), as well as a wide range of different error metrics. Similar to prior sample-
932   complexity results, we investigate the influence of different coverage constraints on regret guarantees.
933   For our initial results (Propositions 3.1, 3.2 and 4.1) we use the condition min(s,a) D(s, a) > 0. Since
934   we assume that all states of our MDPs are reachable, this is equivalent to a full coverage condition
935   (see Table 1 of Uehara & Sun (2021) for an overview of different coverage conditions). We then
936   relax the constraints to partial coverage constraints and prove several negative results (Proposition 3.3
937   and theorems 4.2 and 6.1). Finally, we fully generalize our results from Propositions 3.1 to 3.3
938   and corollary 3.4 into a single theorem (Theorem 3.5) which allows us to determine the worst-case
939
      safety of arbitrary data distributions. To the best of our knowledge, we are the first work to achieve
      such a level of generality.
940
941
      Advancements in Addressing Distribution Shifts Several approaches have been proposed to ad-
942
      dress the issue of out-of-distribution robustness in reward learning, such as ensembles of conservative
943
      reward models (Coste et al., 2023), averaging weights of multiple reward models (Ramé et al., 2024),
944   iteratively updating training labels (Zhu et al., 2024), on-policy reward learning (Lang et al., 2024a),
945   and distributionally robust planning (Zhan et al., 2023).
946
947
      Our work further emphasizes the usefulness of exploring additional assumptions or methods to
      mitigate the perils of distribution shift, as we show that without any additional assumptions, there
948
      are next to no guarantees. We therefore hope that our work can serve as a theoretical baseline, that
949
      people can use to express and analyze their new assumptions or methods.
950
951   In classical machine learning, research in out-of-distribution generalization has a long history, and a
952   rich literature of methods exists (Li et al., 2022; Zhou et al., 2022; Wang et al., 2022a; Liu et al.,
953
      2021; Li et al., 2023; Yoon et al., 2023). These methods could potentially be adapted to address
      distribution shift challenges in reinforcement learning.
954
955
      Contextual Bandits In Section 6 we work in the contextual bandit setting and derive variants of
956
      our results for RLHF. Several theoretical results have been developed that investigate the challenge of
957
      RLHF (Xiong et al., 2024; Zhu et al., 2023; Ji et al., 2023; Mehta et al., 2023) and reward learning in
958   general, (Agarwal et al., 2012; Foster et al., 2020) in the contextual bandit setting. Compared to this
959   prior work, we focus on the offline setting where the data distribution D has been pre-generated by a
960   reference policy.
961
962
963
      B     I NTRODUCTION
964
      B.1   P RELIMINARIES
965
966   A Markov Decision Process (MDP) is a tuple ⟨S, A, τ, µ0 , R, γ⟩ where S is a set of states, A is a
967   set of actions, τ : S×A → ∆(A) is a transition function, µ0 ∈ ∆(S) is an initial state distribution,
968   R : S×A → R is a reward function, and γ ∈ (0, 1) is a discount rate. A policy is a function
969   π : S → ∆(A). A trajectory ξ = ⟨s0 , a0 , s1 , a1 , ...⟩ is a possible path P  in an MDP. The return
                                                                                        ∞
970   function G gives the cumulative discounted reward of a trajectory, G(ξ) = t=0 γ t R(st , at , st+1 ),
971   and the evaluation function J gives the expected trajectory return given a policy, J(π) = Eξ∼π [G(ξ)].
      A policy maximizing J is an optimal policy. The state-action occupancy measure is a function


                                                        18
       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


972
       η : Π → R|S×A| which assigns each policy π ∈ Π a vector of occupancy measure describing the
973                                                                                                   π
974
       P∞ t frequency that a policy takes each action in each state. Formally, η(π)(s, a) = η (s, a) =
       discounted
          t=0 γ · P (st = s, at = a | ξ ∼ π). Note that by writing the reward function R as a vector
975    ⃗ ∈ R|S×A| , we can split J into a linear function of π: J(π) = η π · R.   ⃗ The value function V of a
       R
976    policy encodes the expected future discounted reward from each state when following that policy. We
977    use R to refer to the set of all reward functions. When talking about multiple rewards, we give each
978    reward a subscript Ri , and use Ji , Gi , and Viπ , to denote Ri ’s evaluation function, return function,
979    and π-value function.
980
981    B.2   P ROBLEM FORMALIZATION
982
983    The standard RL process using reward learning works roughly like this:
984
985
             1. You are given a dataset of transition-reward tuples {(si , ai , ri )}ni=0 . Here, each (si , ai ) ∈
                S×A is a transition from some (not necessarily known) MDP ⟨S, A, τ, µ0 , R, γ⟩ that has
986
                been sampled using some distribution D ∈ ∆(S×A), and ri = R(si , ai ). The goal of the
987
                process is to find a policy π̂ which performs roughly optimally for the unknown true reward
988             function R. More formally: JR (π̂) ≈ maxπ∈Π JR (π).
989
990          2. Given some error tolerance ϵ ∈ R, a reward model R̂ : S×A → R is learned using the
991             provided dataset. At
                                  h the end of the learning
                                                      i     process R̂ satisfies some optimality criterion
992             such as: E(s,a)∼D |R̂(s, a) − R(s, a)| < ϵ
993
994          3. The learned reward model R̂ is used to train a policy π̂ that fulfills the following optimality
995
                criterion: π̂ = arg maxπ∈Π JR̂ (π).
996
997
       The problem is that training π̂ to optimize R̂ effectively leads to a distribution shift, as the tran-
       sitions are no longer sampled from the original data distribution D but some other distribution
998
       D̂ (induced by the policy π̂). Depending on the definition of D, this could mean that there are
999
1000
       no guarantees
                 h                   i the expected error of R̂ to the true reward function R is (i.e.,
                       about how close
1001   E(s,a)∼D̂ |R̂(s, a) − R(s, a)| could not be upper-bounded).
1002
       This means that we have no guarantee about the performance of π̂ with respect to the original
1003
       reward function R, so it might happen that π̂ performs arbitrarily bad under the true reward R:
1004
       JR (π̂) ≪ maxπ JR (π).
1005
1006   If for a given data distribution D there exists a reward model R̂ such that R̂ is close in expectation to
1007   the true reward function R but it is possible to learn a policy that performs badly under JR despite
1008   being optimal for R̂, we say that D allows for error-regret mismatch and that R̂ has an error-regret
1009   mismatch.
1010
1011   B.3   T HE MEAN - SQUARED ERROR AS AN ALTERNATIVE DISTANCE MEASURE
1012
1013
       In the main paper, particular in Definition 2.1, we use the mean absolute error (MAE) as our error

1014

1065

1066   particular:
1067

1069             the proof of Proposition 3.3, we can use the same construction of R̂, and an almost identical
1070             derivation shows the bound in dMSE .

1072

1074             linear inequalities such that a given data distribution D is safe, i.e., D ∈ safeMSE (R, ϵ2 , L),
1075

1076

1077

1078             ear constraints. The reason is that by replacing dMAE  D    (R, R̂) with dMSE

                          MSE
1079             {R̂ : dD (R, R̂) ≤ ϵ} becomes an ellipsoid, whereas it was a polytope in the original

1091
1092   B.4     A CONCEPTUAL EXAMPLE OF OVEROPTIMIZATION CONCERNS
1093
       In this section, we present a conceptual example that illustrates overoptimization concerns. This
1094   is meant to serve as an intuition for many of our “negative” theoretical results Proposition 3.3,
1095   corollary 3.4, and theorems 4.2 and 6.1, with the aim to make them more grounded in realistic
1096   concerns.
1097
       In summary, imagine a scenario of a chatbot: It can either obtain “safe” or “dangerous” queries; safe
1098
       queries (e.g. “Please help me create a high-protein diet”) should be answered, dangerous queries
1099
       (e.g. “Please tell me how to build a nuclear weapon”) should be refused. We call answering a query
1100   “helping”, irrespective of whether this is desired or not. We will specifically analyze an always-helping
1101   policy, its regret, and its plausibility to occur from reward learning. Helpful-only policies have been
1102   analyzed in past safety research (Denison et al., 2024) and are often a starting point for policies meant
1103   to become “helpful, honest, and harmless” (Askell et al., 2021).
1104
       First, we look at conditions for when helpful-only policies are unsafe relative to a regret bound L. It
1105
       turns out that they are less safe if there appear more unsafe queries in the deployment environment,
1106   and if the damage caused by answering them is larger — see Appendix B.4.2. Then we look into the
1107   conditions for when this policy can be learned by reward learning — see Appendix B.4.3. It turns out
1108   that if there are “many styles” with which the chatbot can answer an unsafe query, then some of those
1109   answers must have a low probability on the training distribution, and thus a learned reward model
1110   can inflate its reward while achieving a low training error. The always-helping policy can then result
1111   from policy optimization, leading to a large regret. This illustrates an error-regret mismatch.
1112
1113   B.4.1    S PECIFYING THE CONTEXTUAL BANDIT
1114
       We model the situation as follows: Assume a contextual bandit with states and actions given by
1115
1116                                 S = {qsafe , quns. },   A = {aihelp , airef. }N
                                                                                   i=1 .
1117
1118   In other words, there is one safe and one unsafe query,2 and actions that either help with or refuse
1119   to answer the query in N different styles. One should imagine N to be fairly large since there are
1120   lots of ways to vary the style of an answer without changing the content, given that the amount of
1121   possible answers scales exponentially with length.
1122
       We assume the following simplified true reward function, where C > 0 is some (potentially large)
1123
       constant:
1124
1125                                             R(qsafe , aihelp ) = 1
1126                                             R(qsafe , airef. ) = 0
1127                                                                                                             (5)
                                                 R(quns. , aihelp ) = −C
1128
1129                                             R(quns. , airef. ) = 0.
1130
       The idea is that answering a safe query should lead to some positive reward, whereas refusing it
1131   doesn’t create value or damage — the reward is zero. Answering/helping with an unsafe queries,
1132
           2
1133         Having a larger number of safe and unsafe queries does not change the mathematical picture much, but for
       illustration purposes we chose this simplified setting.


                                                             21
       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


1134
       however, incurs a large negative reward −C since it can lead to substantial damage, whereas, once
1135   again, refusing to answer does neither create value nor damage.
1136
1137   Finally, we assume some “true” distribution of queries, given by µuns. ∈ [0, 1] and µsafe = 1 − µuns. .
1138
       These can be imagined to be the frequencies with which actual users in the deployment environment
       ask safe vs. unsafe queries. In total, we have thus specified a contextual bandit (S, A, R, µ).
1139
1140   We now make a regret-analysis — analyzing when an always-helping policy is safe — followed by a
1141   reward learning analysis — under which conditions can an always-helping policy result from reward
1142   learning?
1143
1144   B.4.2    R EGRET ANALYSIS FOR ALWAYS - HELPING POLICY
1145
       For a policy π̂ with answer probabilities π̂(a | q), the policy evaluation (i.e., expected reward) is
1146   given by
1147
                                       N                                                  N
1148                                   X                                                  X
                   JR (π̂) = µsafe ·         π̂(aihelp | qsafe ) − (1 − µsafe ) · C ·           π̂(aihelp | quns. ).    (6)
1149
                                       i=1                                                i=1
1150
1151   This follows directly from (5). The idea is that under a safe query, which happens with probability
1152
       µsafe , the reward is the probability to help with the query. For an unsafe query, which happens with
       probability 1 − µsafe , the reward is −C times the probability that the model helps with that query.
1153
1154   Now, the highest expected reward JR can be achieved if π̂ always helps with a safe query and never
1155   helps with an unsafe query. This is hard to achieve in practice since training the model to refuse
1156   unsafe queries often leads to “over-refusal” on safe queries (Cui et al., 2024). In contrast, the lowest
1157   expected reward JR is achieved is π̂ never helps with a safe query and always helps with an unsafe
1158
       query. Thus, the maximum and minimum expected values are given by:
1159                                          max JR (π̂) = µsafe ,
                                                π̂

1161
                                              min JR (π̂) = −(1 − µsafe ) · C.

1163   Now, for purposes of illustration we look at one specific type of policy π̂: one that always helps.
1164   Let π̂ be such a policy. There are several such policies since they can differ in their allocation of
1165   probabilities to answers of different styles, but the defining property is that their action probabilities
1166
       for helpful answers sum to 1:

1184

1185

1205
       Figure 3: In our conceptual example, we analyze when an always-helping policy π̂ is unsafe. This
1206   depends on the probability of an unsafe query µuns. . For a given damage C of answering such a
1207   query and a given regret bound L, π̂ has a regret of at least L if µuns. is larger than the plotted
1208   µC                                C
        uns. (L) = L/[(1 − L) · C + L]. µuns. (L) grows with growing L and shrinks with growing C.
1209
1210
1211   In Figure 3 we analyze for several different values of the damage C the relationship between the
1212   regret bound L and the smallest probability µCuns. (L) := L/[(1 − L) · C + L] of the unsafe query for
1213   which the policy π̂ would have a regret of at least L. We observe the following:
1214
               • For each C, as the regret bound L gets larger, one needs a larger probability µuns. for π̂ to
1215
                 have regret at least L. This makes sense: π̂ acts correctly on safe queries, and so only unsafe
1216             queries can contribute to the regret. Thus, the more unsafe queries the policy encounters,
1217             the larger its regret becomes.
1218
               • For each regret bound L, as the damage of helping with an unsafe query, C, gets larger, a
1219
                 smaller probability µuns. is sufficient for π̂ to reach regret at least L. This makes sense since
1220             the policy’s overall performance is then more and more dominated by its performance on
1221             unsafe queries.
1222
1223   Note that over time, language models are approaching more concerning “dangerous capabili-
1224   ties” (Phuong et al., 2024; Anthropic, 2024), which means that the caused damage C for following
1225   through with unsafe requests can be imagined to go up over time with increased capabilities. Positive
1226   value goes up, too, but plausibly in the near-term not as fast as the tailrisks. Thus, we can reasonably
1227   think that even for large values of the regret bound L, a small probability µuns. of an unsafe query
1228
       would already cause the always-helping policy π̂ to have a regret of at least L, and thus to be unsafe.
1229   Alternatively, instead of looking at regret, we could also think directly about the expected value
1230   JR (π̂) computed in (8). Then we might say: the policy is unsafe if its expected value is negative, i.e.,
1231   it causes more damage than it provides value. With growing damage C for more capable models, the
1232   expected value eventually becomes negative, and so also this viewpoint suggests that π̂ is not a safe
1233   policy.
1234
1235
       B.4.3    R EWARD LEARNING ANALYSIS
1236   Now, lets assume that the relationship between L, C, and µuns. as per eq. (10) is such that an
1237   always-helping policy π̂ is unsafe, i.e., has regret at least L. Now the question becomes: Under what
1238   conditions could such a policy be learned by reward learning followed by policy optimization? To
1239   be clear, there are also other policies that have regret at least L (e.g., a policy that doesn’t help for
1240   safe queries and always helps for unsafe queries is even worse), but since we are operating under a
1241   worst-case framework under the policy optimization, it is already bad if any always-helping policy π̂
       can be learned. Thus, we are searching for sufficient conditions for this to happen.


                                                                          23
       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


1242
       Thus, let R̂ be the learned reward function. For this to give rise to the policy π̂ under unregularized
1243
       policy optimization, R̂ needs to favor at least one helpful answers over every refusing answer for
1244
       both queries:
1245
1246                                     ∃i∀j : R̂(qsafe , aihelp ) > R̂(qsafe , ajref. ),
1247                                                                                                                (11)
                                         ∃i∀j : R̂(quns. , aihelp ) > R̂(quns. , ajref. ).
1248
1249   Again, since we are operating under a worst-case framework, it is enough if we find one specific
1250   learned reward function with these conditions that can be learned in practice. Thus, for simplicity,
1251   we assume R̂(qsafe , aihelp ) = 1, R̂(qsafe , airef. ) = 0 for all i. Also assume R̂(quns. , airef. ) = 0 for all
1252
       i. Assume there exists a single i0 with B := R̂(quns. , aihelp 0
                                                                          ) > 0, and that R̂(quns. , aihelp ) = −C for
1253
       all i ̸= i0 . Then the conditions from (11) are met, and the learned reward function almost everywhere
1254   agrees with the true reward function R from (5).
1255
1256   Now we want to determine the (mean absolute) training error of this reward model. For this, assume
1257
       we train on some data distribution D ∈ ∆(S × A), given by D(q, a) = D(q) · D(a | q).3 Since our
1258   reward model equals the true reward function in every query-answer pair except (quns. , aihelp
                                                                                                  0
                                                                                                      ), the
1259   training error becomes:

                                                                            0
                                                                                )·     .

                                            range R                                1+C
1263
       Assume we train until we have achieved a small but realistic training error ϵ. Then the question is
1264
       under what conditions R̂ can “slip through” the training by leading to an error bounded above by ϵ.
1265
       This is the case if:
1266                                                         (1 + C) · ϵ

1270   policy π̂ results. Now, note that if the number of “styles” i = 1, . . . , N is very large relative to the
1271

1277
       A note on regularized policy optimization: Regularization can prevent π̂ from being learned even
1278
       if R̂ favors this policy. However, if B = R̂(quns. , aihelp
                                                                0
                                                                   ) > 0 is very large, then this creates so
1279
       much reward that the regularization effect with constant regularization strength can be counteracted.
1280
       Growing B just leads to the need for larger N in (13), and so we can say: If the number of styles N
1281
       is large enough (leading to a small training-probability of some bad action) and the always-helping
1282   policy π̂ has regret larger then L, then supervised reward learning up to reasonable errors ϵ followed
1283   by (un)regularized policy optimization can result in a policy with regret ≥ L. Thus, there is then an
1284   error-regret mismatch, and the distribution D is unsafe, as per Definition 2.1. That a large number of
1285   “bad options” or a small probability of some bad option can lead to an error-regret mismatch is the
1286   core intuition behind our negative results Proposition 3.3, corollary 3.4, and theorems 4.2 and 6.1.
1287
1288
       C     E XISTENCE OF ERROR - REGRET MISMATCH
1289
1290
       In this section, we answer the question under which circumstances error-regret mismatch could
1291
       occur. We consider multiple different settings, starting from very weak statements, and then steadily
1292   increasing the strength and generality.
1293
1294       3
             D(qsafe ) is not necessarily equal to µsafe , the likelihood of safe queries in the deployment environment.
1295   This is intuitive: Before deploying a chatbot in the real world, it may be hard to know what proportion of requests
       will be safe, and the proportion during training may be different.


                                                                24
       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


1296
       C.1   A SSUMPTIONS
1297
1298   For every MDP ⟨S, A, τ, µ0 , R, γ⟩ that we will define in the following statements, we assume the
1299   following properties:
1300
1301            • Finiteness: Both the set of states S and the set of actions A are finite
1302
                • Reachability: Every state in the given MDP’s is reachable, i.e., for every state s ∈ S, there
1303
                  exists a path of transitions from some initial state s0 (s.t. µ0 (s0 ) > 0) to s, such that every
1304
                  transition (s, a, s) in this path has a non-zero probability, i.e., τ (s′ |s, a) > 0. Note that this
1305              doesn’t exclude the possibility of some transitions having zero probability in general.
1306
1307
1308   C.2   I NTUITIVE UNREGULARIZED EXISTENCE STATEMENT
1309
       Definition C.1 (Regret). We define the regret of a policy π with respect to reward function R as

1316

1320

1331   Dπ̂ (s, a) > 0. In other words, there is a “bad” policy for R that is not very supported by D. Then,
