1410   C.3   G ENERAL EXISTENCE STATEMENTS
1411
       We start by giving some definitions:
1412
1413   Definition C.8 (Minkowski addition). Let A, B be sets of vectors, then the Minkowski addition of
1414   A, B is defined as:
1415
                                    A + B := {a + b | a ∈ A, b ∈ B}.
1416
       (Karwowski et al., 2023) showed in their proposition 1, that for every MDP, the corresponding
1417
       occupancy measure space Ω forms a convex polytope. Furthermore, for each occupancy measure
1418   η ∈ Ω there exists at least one policy π η such that ∀(s, a) ∈ S×A, η π (s, a) = η(s, a) (see Theorem
1419   6.9.1, Corollary 6.9.2, and Proposition 6.9.3 of (Puterman, 1994)). In the following proofs, we will
1420   refer multiple times to vertices of the occupancy measure space Ω whose corresponding policies have
1421   high regret. We formalize this in the following definition:
1422   Definition C.9 (High regret vertices). Given a lower regret bound L ∈ [0, 1], an MDP
1423   ⟨S, A, τ, µ0 , R, γ⟩ and a corresponding occupancy measure Ω, we define the set of high-regret
1424   vertices of Ω, denoted by VRL , to be the set of vertices v of Ω for which RegR (π v ) ≥ L
1425
       Definition C.10 (Active inequalities). Let ⟨S, A, τ, µ0 , R, γ⟩ be an MDP with corresponding occu-
1426
       pancy measure space Ω. For every η ∈ Ω, we define the set of transitions (s, a) for which η(s, a) = 0
1427   by zeros(η).
1428
       Definition C.11 (Normal cone). The normal cone of a convex set C ⊂ Rn at point x ∈ C is defined
1429
       as:
1430
                           NC (x) := {n ∈ Rn | nT · (x′ − x) ≤ 0 for all x′ ∈ C}                  (15)
1431
1432   We first state a theorem from prior work that we will use to prove some lemmas in this section:
1433
       Theorem C.12 ( (Schlaginhaufen & Kamgarpour, 2023)). Let ⟨S, A, τ, µ0 , γ⟩ be an MDP without
1434
       reward function and denote with Ω its corresponding occupancy measure space. Then, for every
1435   reward function R and occupancy measure η ∈ Ω, it holds that:
1436
1437                                η is optimal for R    ⇐⇒      R ∈ NΩ (η),                             (16)
1438   where the normal cone is equal to:
1439
1440                               NΩ (η) = Φ + cone {−es,a }(s,a)∈zeros(η)                               (17)
1441   where Φ is the linear subspace of potential functions used for reward-shaping, and the addition is
1442   defined as the Minkowski addition.
1443
1444   Proof. This is a special case of theorem 4.5 of Schlaginhaufen & Kamgarpour (2023), where we
1445   consider the unconstrained- and unregularized RL problem.
1446
1447   From the previous lemma, we can derive the following corollary which uses the fact that Ω is a closed,
1448   and bounded convex polytope (see Proposition 1 of Karwowski et al. (2023)).
1449
       Corollary C.13. Given an MDP ⟨S, A, τ, µ0 , R, γ⟩ and a corresponding occupancy measure space
1450
       Ω, then for every reward function R̂ : S×A → R, and lower regret bound L ∈ [0, 1], the following
1451
       two statements are equivalent:
1452
1453         a) There exists an optimal policy π̂ for R̂ such that π̂ has regret at least L w.r.t. the original
1454            reward function, i.e., RegR (π̂) ≥ L.
1455
                            S
1456         b) R̂ ∈ Φ +        cone {−es,a }(s,a)∈zeros(v) , where Φ is the linear subspace of potential
1457                       v∈VRL
                functions used for reward-shaping, the addition is defined as the Minkowski addition.


                                                         27
       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


1458
       Proof. Let R̂ be chosen arbitrarily. Statement a) can be formally expressed as:
1459
1460                                 ∃π̂ ∈ Π, RegR̂ (π̂) = 0 ∧ RegR (π̂) ≥ L.
1461
1462
       Using Theorem C.12, it follows that:
1463               ∃π̂ ∈ Π, RegR̂ (π̂) = 0 ∧ RegR (π̂) ≥ L
1464
1465                                            ⇐⇒         ∃π̂ ∈ Π,     R̂ ∈ NΩ (η π̂ ) ∧ RegR (π̂) ≥ L
                                                                        [
1466                                            ⇐⇒         R̂ ∈                NΩ (η).
1467                                                              η: RegR (π η )≥L
1468
1469   It remains to be shown that the union in the previous derivation is equivalent to a union over just all
1470
       VRL . First, note that by definition of the set of high-regret vertices VRL (see Definition C.9), it trivially
       holds that:
1471                                      [                       [
                                                NΩ (v) ⊆                   NΩ (η),                              (18)
1472
                                        v∈VRL                η: RegR (π η )≥L
1473
1474   Next, because Ω is a convex polytope, it can be defined as the intersection of a set of defining
1475   half-spaces which are defined by linear inequalities:
1476                                   Ω = {η | aTi · η ≤ bi , for i = 1, ..., m}.
1477
1478   By defining the active index set of a point η ∈ Ω as IΩ (η) = {ai | aTi · η = bi }, Rockafellar & Wets
1479
       (2009) then show that:
                           n                                                                        o
1480
               NΩ (η) = y1 · a1 + ... + ym · am | yi ≥ 0 for i ∈ IΩ (η), yi = 0 for i ∈     / IΩ (η) ,   (19)
1481
1482   (see their theorem 6.46). Note that, because Ω lies in an |S| · (|A| − 1) dimensional affine subspace
1483   (see Proposition 1 of (Karwowski et al., 2023)), a subset of the linear inequalities which define Ω
1484   must always hold with equality, namely, the inequalities that correspond to half-spaces which define
1485   the affine subspace in which Ω resides. Therefore, the corresponding active index set, let’s denote
1486   it by IΩ,Φ (η) because the subspace orthogonal to the affine subspace in which Ω lies corresponds
1487   exactly to Φ, is always non-empty and the same for every η ∈ Ω.
1488   Now, from Equation (19), it follows that for every η ∈ Ω, there exists a vertex v of Ω, such that
1489   NΩ (η) ⊆ NΩ (v). We take this one step further and show that for every η with RegR (π η ) ≥ L,
1490   there must exist a vertex v with RegR (π v ) ≥ L such that NΩ (η) ⊆ NΩ (v). We prove this via case
1491   distinction on η.
1492
1493          • η is in the interior of Ω. In this case, the index set IΩ (η) reduces to IΩ,Φ (η) and because we
1494            have IΩ,Φ (η) ⊆ IΩ (η) for every η ∈ Ω, the claim is trivially true.
1495
              • η itself is already a vertex in which case the claim is trivially true.
1496
1497          • η is on the boundary of Ω. In this case η can be expressed as the convex combination of
1498            some vertices Vη which lie on the same face of Ω as η. Note that all occupancy measures
1499            with regret ≥ L must lie on one side of the half-space defined by the equality RT · η =
1500            L · η min + (1 − L) · η max , where η min and η max are worst-case and best-case occupancy
1501            measures. By our assumption, η also belongs to this side of the half-space. Because η lies in
1502            the interior of the convex hull of the vertices Vη , at least one v ∈ Vη must therefore also lie
1503            on this side of the hyperplane and have regret ≥ L. Because v and η both lie on the same
1504            face of Ω, we have IΩ (η) ⊂ IΩ (v) and therefore also NΩ (η) ⊆ NΩ (v).
1505
1506
       Hence, it must also hold that:
                                                [                         [
1507                                                       NΩ (η) ⊆              NΩ (v),
1508                                    η: RegR (π η )≥L                 v∈VRL
1509
1510   which, together with Equation (18) proves the claim.

1553   Corollary C.13 describes the set of reward functions R̂ for which there exists an optimal policy π̂
1554

1628   In the next few subsections, we provide a more interpretable version of the linear system of inequalities
1629   in Equation (20), and the conditions for when it is solvable and when not.
1630
1631   C.3.1    M ORE INTERPRETABLE STATEMENT
1632
       Ideally, we would like to have a more interpretable statement about which classes of data distributions
1633
       D fulfill the condition of Equation (20). We now show that for an arbitrary MDP and data distribution
1634
       D, D is a safe distribution, i.e., error-regret mismatch is not possible, if and only if D fulfills a fixed
1635   set of linear constraints (independent of D).
1636
       Theorem C.16. For all MDPs ⟨S, A, τ, µ0 , R, γ⟩ and L ∈ [0, 1], there exists a matrix M such that
1637
       for all ϵ > 0 and D ∈ ∆(S×A) we have:
1638
1639                          D ∈ safe(R, ϵ, L)       ⇐⇒        M · D > ϵ · range R · 1,                      (29)
1640   where we use the vector notation of D, and 1 is a vector containing all ones.
1641
1642   Proof. Remember from Lemma C.15, that a data distribution D is safe, i.e., D ∈ safe(R, ϵ, L), if
1643   and only if for all unsafe vertices v ∈ VRL the following system of linear inequalities:
1644
                                            U (v)                    b(v)
1645                                                  ·B ≤                                        (30)
                                        C · diag (D)           ϵ · range R · 1
1646
1647   has no solution. Let v ∈ VRL be chosen arbitrarily and define Uv := {B ∈ R|S×A| | U (v)·B ≤ b(v)},
1648   i.e., Uv is the set of all B ∈ R|S×A| , such that R̂ := R + B has an optimal policy with regret at least
1649   L. Then, Equation (30) has no solution if and only if:
1650
                                      ∀B ∈ Uv ,     C · diag (D) · B ≰ ϵ · range R · 1                        (31)
1651
                                                            T
1652                           ⇐⇒     ∀B ∈ Uv ,     abs(B) · D > ϵ · range R,                                 (32)
1653   where we used the definition of the matrices C, and diag (D) (see Equation (25)) and abs(·) denotes
1654   the element-wise absolute value function. Now, we will finish the proof by showing that there exists
1655   a finite set of vectors X ⊂ Uv (which is independent of the choice of D), such that for every x ∈ X,
1656   Equation (32) holds if and only if it is true for all B, i.e., more formally:
1657
                                          ∀B ∈ X,      abs(B)T · D > ϵ · range R
1658
1659                              ⇐⇒      ∀B ∈ Uv ,    abs(B)T · D > ϵ · range R.
1660   And since X is finite, we can then summarize the individual elements of X as rows of a matrix M
1661   and get the desired statement by combining the previous few statements, namely:
1662
                               D ∈ safe(R, ϵ, L)      ⇐⇒        M · D > ϵ · range R · 1                       (33)
1663
1664
       Towards this goal, we start by reformulating Equation (32) as a condition on the optimal value of a
1665   convex optimization problem:
1666
1667                ∀x ∈ Uv , abs(x)T · D > ϵ · range R

1668
            ⇐⇒        min abs(x)T · D > ϵ · range R
1669                  x∈Uv
1670        ⇐⇒      abs(x∗ )T · D > ϵ · range R,      where x∗ :=           arg min       abs(x)T · D
                                                                                 x∈Uv
1671
1672        ⇐⇒      abs(x∗ )T · D > ϵ · range R,       where x∗ :=          arg min       abs(x)T · D,        (34)
                                                                                  x
1673
                                                                             subject to    U (v) · x ≤ b(v)


                                                           31
       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


1674
       Note that the optimal value x∗ of this convex optimization problem depends on the precise definition
1675   of the data distribution D. But importantly, the set over which we optimize (i.e., Uv defined as the
1676   set of all x, such that U (v) · x ≤ b) does not depend on D! The goal of this part of the proof is
1677   to show that for all possible D the optimal value of the optimization problem in Equation (34) is
1678   always going to be one of the vertices of Uv . Therefore, we can transform the optimization problem
1679   in Equation (34) into a new optimization problem that does not depend on D anymore. It will then be
1680   possible to transform this new optimization problem into a simple set of linear inequalities which
1681   will form the matrix M in Equation (33).
1682   Towards that goal, we continue by splitting up the convex optimization problem into a set of
1683   linear programming problems. For this, we partition R|S×A| into its different orthants Oc for
1684   c ∈ {−1, 1}|S×A| (a high-dimensional generalization of the quadrants). More precisely, for every
1685   x ∈ Oc , we have diag (c) · x = abs(x). Using this definition, we can reformulate the constraint on
1686   the convex optimization problem as follows:
1687
1688                                 min        (diag (c) · xc )T · D > ϵ · range R,                       (35)
                                c∈{−1,1}|S×A|

1691
1692                                    xc := arg min        (diag (c) · x)T · D                           (36)
                                                         x

1694                                            subject to       U (v) · x ≤ b(v)
1695                                                          diag (c) · x ≥ 0,
1696
       or xc := ∅ in case the linear program is infeasible. Finally, by re-parametrizing each linear program
1697   using the variable transform x′ = diag (c) · x we can convert these linear programs into standard
1698   form:
1699
1700                 xc := diag (c) ·      arg min
                                                ′
                                                                          x′T · D                          (37)
                                                x
1701
                                           subject to         U (v) · diag (c) · x′ ≤ b(v)
1702
1703
                                                                               x′ ≥ 0,
1704                                                −1
       where we used twice the fact that diag (c) = diag (c), and hence, x = diag (c) · x′ . Because it was
1705   possible to transform these linear programming problems described in Equation (36) into standard
1706   form using a simple variable transform, we can apply standard linear programming theory to draw
1707   the following conclusions (see Theorem 3.4 and Section 6 of Chapter 2 of (Vanderbei, 1998) for
1708   reference):
1709
1710         1. The set of constraints in Equations (36) and (37) are either infeasible or they form a
1711            polyhedral set of feasible solutions.
1712
1713
             2. If the set of constraints in Equations (36) and (37) are feasible, then there exists an optimal
                feasible solution that corresponds to one of the vertices (also called basic feasible solutions)
1714
                of the polyhedral constraint sets. This follows from the fact that the objective function is
1715
                bounded from below by zero.
1716
1717
       Let’s denote the polyhedral set of feasible solutions defined by the constraints in Equation (36) by
1718   Fc (v). Because Fc (v) does not depend on the specific choice of the data distribution, this must mean
1719   that for every possible data distribution D, we have either xc = ∅ or xc is one of the vertices of
1720   Fc (v), denoted by vertices(Fc (v))! Note that, by definition of xc , it holds that:
1721
1722                  ∀x ∈ vertices(Fc (v)),        (diag (c) · xc )T · D ≤ (diag (c) · x)T · D.           (38)
1723   Therefore, we can define:
1724                                                                                                    

                                                                                                        (39)

       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


1728
       where MX (v) contains the element-wise absolute value of all vectors of X(v) as row vectors. Let D
1729   be an arbitrary data distribution. Then, we’ve shown the following equivalences:
1730

1752   C.3.2    D ERIVING THE CONDITIONS ON D
1753
1754
       In Theorem C.16 we’ve shown that there exists a set of linear constraints M · D > ϵ · range R · 1,
       such that whenever a data distribution D satisfies these constraints, it is safe. In this subsection, we
1755
       derive closed-form expressions for the individual rows of M to get a general idea about the different
1756
       factors determining whether an individual data distribution is safe.
1757
1758   In the proof of Theorem C.16, we showed that M has the form:
1759
                                                             
                                                    abs(x1 )T
1760                                                    ..
                                            M =              ,
                                                             
1761                                                     .
1762                                                          abs(xl )T
1763
       for some set X = {x1 , ..., xl }, where each x ∈ X belongs to a vertex of the set of linear constraints
1764   defined by the following class of system of linear inequalities:
1765
1766               U (v)            b(v)        (Corresponds to the set of unsafe reward functions)
                             ·x≤                                                                          (41)
1767            −diag (c)             0         (Corresponds to the orthant Oc )
1768
       for some v ∈ VRL (the set of unsafe vertices of Ω), and some c ∈ {−1, 1}|S×A| (defining the orthant
1769
       Oc ).
1770
1771   To ease the notation in the following paragraphs, we will use the notation Uv for the polyhedral set of
1772   x such that U (v) · x ≤ b(v), and Fc (v) for the set of solutions to the full set of linear inequalities in
1773   Equation (41). Furthermore, we will use n := |S| and m := |A|.
1774   We start by giving a small helper definition.
1775   Definition C.17 (General position, (Stanley, 2024)). Let H be a set of hyperplanes in Rn . Then H is
1776   in general position if:
1777
1778                    {H1 , ..., Hp } ⊆ H, p ≤ n            =⇒    dim(H1 ∩ ... ∩ Hp ) = n − p
1779                    {H1 , ..., Hp } ⊆ H, p > n            =⇒    H1 ∩ ... ∩ Hp = ∅
1780
1781   We will use this definition in the next few technical lemmas. First, we claim that each of the vertices
       of Fc (v) must lie on the border of the orthant Oc .


                                                               33
       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


1782
       Lemma C.18 (Vertices lie on the intersection of the two constraint sets.). All vertices of the polyhedral
1783   set, defined by the system of linear inequalities:
1784
1785                                             U (v)           b(v)
                                                            ·x≤                                             (42)
1786                                           −diag (c)           0
1787
       must satisfy some of the inequalities of −diag (c) · x ≤ 0 with equality.
1788
1789
1790
       Proof. Let Uv be the set of solutions of the upper part of the system of linear equations in Equation (42)
       and Oc be the set of solutions of the lower part of the system of linear equations in Equation (42).
1791
       The lemma follows from the fact that Uv can be expressed as follows (see Equation (27) and the
1792
       subsequent paragraph):
1793
1794                             Uv = −R + Φ + cone {−es,a }(s,a)∈zeros(v) ,                                 (43)
1795
       where Φ is a linear subspace. Hence, for every x that satisfies the constraints U (v) · x ≤ b(v), x lies
1796
       on the interior of the line segment spanned between x′ = x + ϕ, and x′′ = x − ϕ for some ϕ ∈ Φ,
1797
       ϕ ̸= 0. Note that every point on this line segment also satisfies the constraints U (v) · x ≤ b(v).
1798   Therefore, x can only be a vertex if it satisfies some of the additional constraints, provided by the
1799   inequalities −diag (c) · x ≤ 0, with equality.
1800
1801   Consequently, every vertex of Fc (v) is the intersection of some k-dimensional surface of Uv and
1802   k > 0 standard hyperplanes (hyperplanes whose normal vector belongs to the standard basis).
1803
       Lemma C.19 (Basis for Φ. (Schlaginhaufen & Kamgarpour, 2023)). The linear subspace Φ of
1804
       potential shaping transformations can be defined as:
1805
1806                                          Φ = span(A − γ · P ),
1807
1808
       where A, P ∈ R(n·m)×n for n = |S|, m = |A| are matrices defined as:
                        m
1809                    1     0m · · · 0m
                                                
                                                                   τ ( · | s1 , a1 )
                                                                                               
                          m     m           m

                  A := 
                       · · · · · · . . . · · ·  , P :=                                         ,
                                                                                               
1811                                            
                                                           ···            ···               ··· 
1812                      m     m           m                     τ ( · | sn , am )
                        0     0     ··· 1
1813
1814   where 0m , 1m are column vectors and τ (·|si , aj ) is a row vector of the form
1815   [τ (s1 | si , aj ), · · · , τ (sn | si , aj )].
1816
1817
       Furthermore, we have dim Φ = n.
1818
1819
       Proof. This has been proven by (Schlaginhaufen & Kamgarpour, 2023) (see their paragraph "Iden-
1820
       tifiability" of Section 4). The fact that dim Φ = n follows from the fact that Φ is the linear space
1821
       orthogonal to the affine space containing the occupancy measure space Ω, i.e. Φ⊥ = L where
1822   L is the linear subspace parallel to span(Ω) (see the paragraph Convex Reformulation of Section
1823   3 of (Schlaginhaufen & Kamgarpour, 2023)) and the fact that dim span(Ω) = n · (m − 1) (see
1824   Proposition 1 of (Karwowski et al., 2023)).
1825
1826   Lemma C.20 (Dimension of Uv ). dim Uv = n · m.
1827
1828   Proof. Remember that Uv can be expressed as follows (see Equation (27) and the subsequent
1829   paragraph):
1830                        Uv = −R + Φ + cone {−es,a }(s,a)∈zeros(v) ,                     (44)
1831   From Lemma C.19 we know that dim Φ = n. We will make the argument that:
1832

1833         a) dim cone {−es,a }(s,a)∈zeros(v) ≥ n · (m − 1)
1834
1835         b) There exist exactly n · (m − 1) basis vectors of cone {−es,a }(s,a)∈zeros(v) such that the
                combined set of these vectors and the basis vectors of Φ is linearly independent.


                                                          34
       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


1836
       From this, it must follow that:
1837                  h                              i      h i
1838            dim Φ + cone {−es,a }(s,a)∈zeros(v)    = dim Φ + n · (m − 1) = n · m
1839
1840   For a), remember that v is a vertex of the occupancy measure space Ω and that each vertex v of Ω
1841   corresponds to at least one deterministic policy π v (see Proposition 1 of (Karwowski et al., 2023)).
1842   And since every deterministic policy is zero for exactly n · (m − 1) transitions, it must follow that v
1843   is also zero in at least n · (m − 1) transitions, since whenever π v (a|s) = 0 for some (s, a) ∈ S×A,
1844   we have:
1845                    ∞
                        X                                                   ∞
                                                                            X
1846   v(s, a) =            γ t ·P (st = s, at = a | π v , τ ) = π v (a|s)·     γ t ·P (st = s | π v , τ ) = 0.
1847                   t=0                                                  t=0

1848   Therefore, it follows that dim cone {−es,a }(s,a)∈zeros(v) ≥ n · (m − 1).
1849
1850
       For b), (Puterman, 1994) give necessary and sufficient conditions for a point x ∈ Rn·m to be part of
       Ω (see the dual linear program in section 6.9.1 and the accompanying explanation), namely:
1851                                        h                                         i
1852                       x ∈ Ω ⇐⇒           (A − γ · P )T · x = µ0 and I · x ≥ 0 ,
1853
1854   where I is the identity matrix and we use the vector notation of the initial state distribution µ0 .
1855   Because v is a vertex of Ω, it can be described as the intersection of n · m supporting hyperplanes of
1856   Ω that are in general position. Because (A − γ · P ) has rank n (see Lemma C.19), this must mean
1857
       that for v at least n · (m − 1) inequalities of the system I · v ≥ 0 hold with equality and the combined
       set of the corresponding row vectors and the row vectors of (A − γ · P )T is linearly independent (as
1858
       the vectors correspond to the normal vectors of the set of n · m hyperplanes in general position).
1859
1860   Note that the set of unit vectors that are orthogonal to v is precisely defined by {−es,a }(s,a)∈zeros(v) ,
1861   since, by definition of zeros(v) (see Definition C.10), we have
1862
                                     ∀x ∈ {−es,a }(s,a)∈zeros(v) ,    xT · v = 0.
1863
1864   From this, it must follow that the polyhedral set Uv , has dimension n · m.
1865
1866   Lemma C.21 (Defining the faces of Uv ). Each k-dimensional face F of Uv (with k ≥ n) can be
1867   expressed as:
1868                    −R + Φ + cone (EF ) ,           where EF ⊂ {−es,a }(s,a)∈zeros(v) ,                  (45)
1869
       such that |EF | = k − n and the combined set of vectors of EF and the columns of A − γ · P is
1870
       linearly independent.
1871
1872
       Proof. Remember that Uv can be expressed as follows (see Equation (27) and the subsequent
1873
       paragraph):
1874

                               Uv = −R + Φ + cone {−es,a }(s,a)∈zeros(v) ,                        (46)
1875
       This means that we can express Uv as a polyhedral cone, spanned by non-negative combinations of:
1876
1877
              • The column vectors of the matrix A − γ · P .
1878
1879          • The column vectors of the matrix −(A − γ · P ). Since Φ is a linear subspace and a cone
1880            is spanned by only the positive combinations of its set of defining vectors we also have to
1881            include the negative of this matrix to allow arbitrary linear combinations.
1882
              • The set of vectors {−es,a }(s,a)∈zeros(v) .
1883
1884   Consequently, each face of Uv of dimension k is spanned by a subset of the vectors that span Uv
1885   and is therefore also a cone of these vectors. Because the face has dimension k, we require exactly
1886   k linearly independent vectors, as it’s not possible to span a face of dimension k with less than k
1887   linearly independent vectors, and every additional linearly independent vector would increase the
1888   dimension of the face. Furthermore, since Φ is a linear subspace that is unbounded by definition, it
1889   must be part of every face. Therefore, every face of Uv has a dimension of at least n (the dimension
       of Φ).


                                                          35
       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


1890
       Note that the converse of Lemma C.21 doesn’t necessarily hold, i.e., not all sets of the form described
1891   in Equation (45) are necessarily surfaces of the polyhedral set U (v) · x ≤ b(v).
1892
1893   We are now ready to develop closed-form expressions for the vertices of Fc (v). Note that it is possible
1894
       for 0 ∈ Rn·m to be a vertex of Fc (v). But in this case, according to Theorem C.16, this must mean
       that the linear system of inequalities M · D > ϵ · range R · 1 is infeasible (since M would contain a
1895
       zero row and all elements on the right-hand side are non-negative), which means that in this case
1896
       safe(R, ϵ, L) = ∅. We will therefore restrict our analysis to all non-zero vertices of Fc (v).
1897
1898
       Proposition C.22 (Vertices of Fc (v).). Every vertex vF G of Fc (v), with vF G ̸= 0, lies on the
       intersection of some face F of the polyhedral set Uv and some face G of the orthant Oc and is defined
1899
       as follows:
1900
                                                                                  −1
1901

                    vF G = −R + [A − γ · P, EF ] · EG · [A − γ · P, EF ]              · EG · R,
1902
1903   where EF , EG are matrices whose columns contain standard unit vectors, such that:
1904
1905                   F     =      −R + Φ + cone (EF ) ,        for EF ⊂ {−es,a }(s,a)∈zeros(v)
1906                   G     =      {x ∈ Rn·m | EG · x = 0}.
1907
1908   Proof. We start by defining the faces of the orthant Oc . Remember that Oc is the solution set to the
1909   system of inequalities diag (c) · x ≥ 0. Therefore, each defining hyperplane of Oc is defined by one
1910   row i of diag (c), i.e. diag (c)i · x = 0. Note that since c ∈ {−1, 1}n·m , this is equivalent to the
1911   equation eTi · x = 0 where ei is either the i’th standard unit vector or its negative. And because every
1912   l-dimensional face G of Oc is the intersection of l standard hyperplanes {ei1 , ..., eil }, this must mean
1913   that G is defined as the set of solutions to the system of equations EG · x = 0 where EG is the matrix
1914   whose row vectors are the vectors {ei1 , ..., eil }.
1915   Next, let vF G be an arbitrary non-zero vertex of Fc (v). As proven in Lemma C.18, every vertex of
1916   Fc (v) must satisfy some of the inequalities diag (c) · x ≥ 0 for c ∈ {−1, 1}n·m with equality. This
1917   means that vF G must lie on some face G of the orthant Oc . The non-zero property guarantees that
1918   not all inequalities of the system of inequalities diag (c) · x ≥ 0 are satisfied with equality, i.e. that G
1919   is not a vertex. Assume that k > 0 inequalities are not satisfied with equality. Therefore, G must
1920   have dimension k, and EG ∈ Rn·m×k .
1921   Since vF G is a vertex of the intersection of the orthant Oc and the polyhedral set Uv , and it only
1922   lies on a k-dimensional face of Oc , it must also lie on a n · m − k dimensional face F of Uv such
1923   that the combined set of hyperplanes defining F and G is in general position. The condition that the
1924   combined set of hyperplanes is in general position is necessary, to guarantee that vF G has dimension
1925   0 and is therefore a proper vertex.
1926
       From Lemma C.21 we know that F can be expressed as:
1927
1928                       −R + Φ + cone (EF ) ,         where EF ⊂ {−es,a }(s,a)∈zeros(v) ,                  (47)
1929
       such that |EF | = n · (m − 1) − k and the combined set of vectors of EF and the columns of A − γ · P
1930
       are linearly independent.
1931
1932   Because vF G is part of both, F and G, we can combine all information that we gathered about F and
1933   G and deduce that it must hold that:
1934         EG · vF G = 0            , and     ∃x ∈ Rn·m−k ,      vF G = −R + [A − γ · P, EF ] · x,          (48)
1935         |     {z    }                      |                        {z                       }
            equivalent to vF G ∈G                                  equivalent to vF G ∈F
1936
1937   where for x in Equation (48) it additionally must hold that ∀i ∈ {n + 1, ..., n · m − k}, xi ≥ 0. This
1938   must hold because these last entries of x should form a convex combination of the vectors in EF (as
1939   F is defined to lie in the cone of EF , see Equation (47)). We briefly state the following two facts that
1940   will be used later in the proof:
1941
1942         a) vF G is the only vector in Rn·m that fulfills both conditions in Equation (48). This is because
1943            we defined F in such a way that the intersection of F and G is a single point. And only
                points in this intersection fulfill both conditions in Equation (48).


                                                            36
       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


1944
             b) For every non-zero vertex vF G , there can only exist a single x that satisfies the two conditions
1945            in Equation (48). This follows directly from the assumption that the combined set of vectors
1946            of EF and the columns of A − γ · P are linearly independent (see Equation (47) and the
1947            paragraph below).
1948
1949   We can combine the two conditions in Equation (48) to get the following, unified condition that is
1950   satisfied for every non-zero vertex vF G :
1951
1952                     ∃x ∈ Rn·m−k , EG · − R + [A − γ · P, EF ] · x = 0n·m−k ,                   (49)
1953
       From this, it is easy to compute the precise coordinates of vF G :
1954
1955
                                                           −1
                              x = EG · [A − γ · P, EF ]        · EG · R                                      (50)
1956
1957
                                                                            −1
                   =⇒ vF G = −R + [A − γ · P, EF ] · EG · [A − γ · P, EF ]     · EG · R.                     (51)
1958
1959
       We finish the proof by showing that the matrix inverse in Equation (50) always exists for every
1960   non-zero vertex vF G . Assume, for the sake of contradiction, that the matrix EG · [A − γ · P, EF ]
1961   is not invertible. We will show that in this case, there exists a z ∈ Rn·m with z ̸= vF G such that z
1962   fulfills both conditions in Equation (48). As we’ve shown above in fact a) this is not possible, hence
1963   this is a contradiction.
1964
       Assuming that EG · [A − γ · P, EF ] is not invertible, we know from standard linear algebra that in
1965
       that case the kernel of this matrix has a dimension larger than zero. Let y1 , y2 , be two elements of
1966   this kernel with y1 ̸= y2 .
1967
1968   Earlier in this proof, we showed that for every non-zero vertex vF G , Equation (49) is satisfiable. Let
1969
       x be a solution to Equation (49). From our assumptions, it follows that both x + y1 and x + y2 must
       also be solutions to Equation (49) as:
1970

1971                ∀y ∈ {y1 , y2 }, EG · −R + [A − γ · P, EF ] · (x + y)
1972
1973                                            =     −EG · R + EG · [A − γ · P, EF ] · (x + y)
1974                                            =     −EG · R + EG · [A − γ · P, EF ] · x
1975

                                                =     EG · − R + [A − γ · P, EF ] · x
1976
1977                                            =     0n·m−k .
1978
1979   And from this, it will follow that both, x + y1 and x + y2 must satisfy both conditions in Equation (48).
1980
       Because x + y1 ̸= x + y2 , it must also hold that:
1981               −R + [A − γ · P, EF ] · (x + y1 )       ̸=    −R + [A − γ · P, EF ] · (x + y2 ),
1982
1983
       see fact b) above for a proof of this. And this would mean that there exists at least one z ∈ Rn·m
       with z ̸= vF G such that z fulfills both conditions in Equation (48). But as we have shown in fact a),
1984
       this is not possible. Therefore, the matrix EG · [A − γ · P, EF ] must be invertible for every non-zero
1985
       vertex vF G .
1986

       We are now ready to provide more specific information about the exact conditions necessary for a
1988   data distribution D to be safe.

       Corollary C.23 (Vertices of Fc (v).). For all ϵ > 0, L ∈ [0, 1] and MDPs ⟨S, A, τ, µ0 , R, γ⟩, there
1990
       exists a matrix M such that:
1991
1992                          D ∈ safe(R, ϵ, L)       ⇐⇒        M · D > ϵ · range R · 1,                     (52)
1993
       for all D ∈ ∆(S×A), where we use the vector notation of D, and 1 is a vector containing all ones.
1994
1995   The matrix M is defined as:                              
1996                                                  abs(x1 )T
1997                                             M =  ··· ,
                                                       abs(xl )T

       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025

       Algorithm 1 Computes the set of conditions used to determine the safety of a data distribution.
1999

2001
        2:    I ← the set of all unit vectors of dimension |S×A|. Create a fixed ordering of S and A and
             denote each vector of I by e(s,a) for a unique tuple (s, a) ∈ S×A.
2002
2003
        3:      candidates ← [ ]
2004    4:      Πd ← Set of deterministic policies of M DP
2005    5:      for π ∈ {π ′ ∈ Πd : RegR (π ′ ) ≥ L} do         ▷ Create a set of potential row candidates.
2006    6:           E ← {e(s,a) ∈ I : π(a|s) = 0}
2007    7:          for EF ⊂ E do
2008    8:              for subset ⊆ I \ EF , |subset| = |S| do
2009    9:                  EG ← EF ∪ subset
2010   10:                  EF , EG ← ColumnMatrix(EF ), RowMatrix(EG )
2011   11:                  candidates.append((EF , EG ))
2012
2013   12:      rows ← [ ]                                ▷ Find the valid rows amongst the candidates
2014   13:      for (EF , EG ) ∈ candidates do
2015
       14:          k ← num_columns(E
                                         F)
2016   15:          if rank EG · [A − γ · P, −EF ] = n + k then
2017                                                −1

       16:              x ← EG · [A − γ · P, −EF ]     · EG · R
2019   17:              if ∀i ∈ {n, n + 1, ..., n + k} xi ≥ 0 then
                                                                    T

2021

       19:                  rows.append row

2026
2027   where an individual row xi of M can either be all zeros, or
2028                                                                         −1
2029                  xi = −R + [A − γ · P, Ei1 ] · Ei2 · [A − γ · P, Ei1 ]     · Ei2 · R,               (53)
2030
       where Ei1 , Ei2 are special matrices whose columns contain standard unit vectors.
2031
2032
       Proof. This is a simple combination of Theorem C.16 and Proposition C.22.
2033
2034
       In particular, Equation (53) shows that whether a particular data distribution D is safe or not depends
2035
       on the true reward function R, as well as the transition distribution τ (encoded by the matrix P ).

       C.3.3     A LGORITHM TO COMPUTE THE CONDITIONS ON D
2038
2039   The derivations of Appendix C.3.2 can be used to define a simple algorithm that constructs matrix
2040   M . An outline of such an algorithm is presented in Algorithm 1. We use the terms RowMatrix and
2041   ColumnMatrix to denote functions that take a set of vectors and arrange them as rows/columns of a
2042   matrix.
2043   To give a brief explanation of the algorithm:
2044
2045           • Line 4 follows from the definitions of VRL , X(v) and X (see Definition C.9 and eqs. (39)
2046             and (40)).
2047           • Line 6 are taken from the definition of EF in Proposition B.20 (except that we don’t take
2048             the negative of the vectors and instead negate EF in the final formula).
2049           • Lines 7 and 8 are taken from the definition of EG (see the first two paragraphs of Propo-
2050             sition C.22). We additionally ensure that EF is a subset of EG as otherwise, the matrix
2051             EG · [A − γ · P, −EF ] is not invertible (due to the multiplication of EG · EF ) and we know
                 that the matrix must be invertible for every vertex.

               • Lines 15 and 17 compute the row of the matrix M . The formulas are a combination of the
2074
                 definition of the sets X(v), X (see Equations (39) and (40)), the matrix MX (Equation (40))
2075             and Proposition C.22.
2076
               • Line 14 checks whether the matrix EG · [A − γ · P, −EF ] is invertible. This is always the
2077
                 case for the rows of M (see the last few paragraphs of the proof of Proposition C.22) but
2078
                 might not be true for other candidates.
2079
2080
               • To explain Line 16, remember that every row of the matrix M corresponds to the element-
                 wise absolute value of a vector that lies on the intersection of two polyhedral sets F , and G
2081
                 (see Proposition C.22). The polyhedral set F is defined via a convex cone. To check that
2082
                 our solution candidate lies in this convex cone, we have to check whether the last entries of
2083             x = (EG · [A − γ · P, −EF ])−1 · EG · R, the entries belonging to the vectors in EF , are
2084             non-negative.
2085
2086   The asymptotic runtime of this naive algorithm is exponential in |S × A| due to the iterations over all
2087   subsets in Lines 6 and 7. However, better algorithms might exist and we consider this an interesting
2088   direction for future work.

       C.3.4      W ORKING EXAMPLE OF COMPUTING MATRIX M
2091   Figure 4 shows a simple toy-MDP with a single state and three actions, for which we then compute
2092   matrix M using Algorithm 1. Due to the simple structure of the MDP, the auxiliary matrix A and the
2093   state-transition matrix P (both used in Algorithm 1) become trivial:
2094

2100   the constraints are over three-dimensional vectors. However, because D is a probability distribution,
2101   it must live in a two-dimensional subspace of this three-dimensional space, and using the identity
2102   d3 = 1 − d1 − d2 we can transform the constraints as follows:

       The brown triangle in Figure 4 depicts the 2d-probability simplex of all distributions over the three
2107   actions of the MDP.
2108
2109   Note that constraint ⃝ a is a redundant constraint that is already covered by the constraint ⃝
                                                                                                    d and the
2110
       border of the simplex. It would therefore be possible to disregard the computation of such constraints
       entirely, which could speed up the execution of Algorithm 1. In the next section, we discuss this
2111
       possibility, as well as other potential directions in which we can extend Theorem 3.5.
2112
2113
2114   C.3.5    B UILDING UP ON T HEOREM 3.5
2115
2116   There are multiple ways how future work can build up on the results of Theorem 3.5:
2117   Finding sufficient conditions for safety that require less information about the true reward
2118   function: It would be very interesting to investigate whether there exists some subset of the set of safe
2119   data distributions for which it is possible to more easily determine membership. This could be helpful
2120   in practice, as knowing that a provided data distribution is safe directly yields safety guarantees for
2121   the resulting optimal policy.
2122   Developing faster methods to construct M: While the algorithm we provide above runs in expo-
2123   nential time it is unclear whether this has to be the case. The set of vectors that are computed by
2124   our algorithm is redundant in the sense that some elements can be dropped as the conditions they
2125   encode are already covered by other rows of M. Depending on what fraction of computed elements
2126   are redundant it might be possible to develop an algorithm that prevents the computation of redundant
2127   rows and can therefore drastically reduce computation time. Alternatively, it would be interesting to
2128   develop fast algorithms to compute only parts of M. This could be especially interesting to quickly
2129
       prove the unsafety of a data distribution, which only requires that a single constraint is violated.
2130   Extending Theorem 3.5 to the regularized policy optimization case: This would allow one to
2131   extend the use case we described above to an even wider variety of reward learning algorithms, such
2132   as RLHF.
2133   A theoretical baseline (a broader view on the previous point): Most of the options above reveal
2134   the properties of the “baseline algorithm” of reinforcement learning under unknown rewards: First, a
2135   reward model is trained, and second, a policy is optimized against the trained reward model. The
2136   matrix M is valid for the simplest such baseline algorithms without any regularization in either the
2137   reward model or the policy. As we mentioned in comments to other reviewers, it would be valuable
2138   to study other training schemes (e.g., regularized reward modeling, or switching back and forth
2139   between policy optimization and reward modeling on an updated data distribution), for which the set
2140   of safe data distributions (or “safe starting conditions”) is likely more favorable than for the baseline
2141
       case. Then, similar to how empirical work compares new algorithms empirically against baseline
       algorithms, we hope our work can be a basis to theoretically study improved RL algorithms under
2142
       unknown rewards, e.g. by deriving a more favorable analog of the matrix M and comparing it with
2143
       our work.
2144
2145
2146   C.4     E XISTENCE OF NEGATIVE RESULTS IN THE RLHF SETTING
2147
2148   C.4.1    G ENERALIZATION OF THE ERROR MEASUREMENT: OVERVIEW
2149
2150   Our results have so far expressed the error of the learned reward R̂ in terms of Equation (1), i.e., in
2151
       terms of the expected error of individual transitions. In this section, we show that many common
       reward learning training objectives can be upper-bounded in terms of the expected error metric
2152
       defined in Equation (1). This in turn means that our negative results generalize to reward learning
2153
       algorithms that use these other training objectives. We state all upper bounds for MDPs with finite
2154   time horizon T (but note that these results directly generalize to MDPs with infinite time horizon by
2155   taking the limit of T → ∞).
2156
2157
       In the finite horizon setting, trajectories are defined as a finite list of states and actions: ξ =
       s0 , a0 , s1 , ..., aT −1 . We use Ξ for the set of all trajectories of length T . As in the previous sections,

       by showing that low expected error in transitions implies low expected error in trajectory returns:

                                          s0 ∼µ0 ,      DKL pR (·|ξ1 , ξ2 )||pR̂ (·|ξ1 , ξ2 ) ,
             s ∈S                     ξ1 ,ξ2 ∼π(s0 )
2355       µ0 (s′ )>0
2356
2357   where we used the fact that the KL divergence is always positive.
2358
2359   C.4.3        RLHF BANDIT FORMULATION
2360
2361   RLHF, especially in the context of large language models, is usually modeled in a contextual bandit
2362   setting ( (Ziegler et al., 2019; Stiennon et al., 2020; Bai et al., 2022; Ouyang et al., 2022; Rafailov
2363   et al., 2023)). A contextual bandit ⟨S, A, µ0 , R⟩ is defined by a set of states S, a set of actions
2364
       A, a data distribution µ0 ∈ ∆(S), and a reward function R : S×A → R. The goal is to learn a
       policy π : S → ∆(A) which maximizes the expected return J(π) = Es∼µ0 ,a∼π(·|s) [R(s, a)]. In the
2365
       context of language models, S is usually called the set of prompts/contexts, and A the set of responses.
2366
       We model the human preference distribution over the set of answers A using the Bradley-Terry model
2367   (Bradley & Terry, 1952). Given a prompt s ∈ S and two answers a1 , a2 ∈ A, then the probability
2368   that a human supervisor prefers answer a1 to answer a2 is modelled as:
2369
2370                                                                         exp(R(s, a1 ))
                                       pR (a1 ≻ a2 | s) =                                            ,                                   (72)
2371                                                                 exp(R(s, a1 )) + exp(R(s, a2 ))
2372
       where R : S×A → R is assumed to be the true, underlying reward function of the human.
2373
2374   RLHF is usually done with the following steps:
2375
               1. Supervised finetuning: Train/Fine-tune a language model πref using supervised training.


                                                                            44
       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


2376
             2. Reward learning: Given a data distribution over prompts µ ∈ ∆(S), use µ and πref to
2377            sample a set of transitions (s, a0 , a1 ) ∈ S×A × A where s ∼ µ and a0 , a1 ∼ πref (·|s). Use
2378
                this set of transitions to train a reward model R̂ which minimizes the following loss:
2379                                                            h                                 i
2380                        LR (R̂) = −E(s,a0 ,a1 ,c)∼µ,πref ,pR log σ(R̂(s, ac ) − R̂(s, a1−c )) ,       (73)
2381
2382            where c ∈ {0, 1} and p(c = 0|s, a0 , a1 ) = pR (a0 ≻ a1 |s).
2383         3. RL finetuning: Use the trained reward model R̂ to further finetune the language model πref
2384            using reinforcement learning. Make sure that the new model does not deviate too much
2385            from the original model by penalizing the KL divergence between the two models. This can
2386            be done by solving the following optimization problem for some λ > 0:
2387                                                   h       i
2388
                           π = arg max Es∼µ,a∼π(·|s) R̂(s, a) − λ · DKL (π(a|s)||πref (a|s))          (74)
                                        π
2389
2390   C.4.4    S AFE AND UNSAFE DATA DISTRIBUTIONS FOR RLHF
2391
       Definition C.30 (Safe- and unsafe data distributions for RLHF). For a given contextual ban-
2392
       dit ⟨S, A, µ0 , R⟩, let ϵ > 0, L ∈ [0, 1], λ ∈ [0, ∞), and πref : S → ∆(A) an arbi-
2393   trary reference policy. Similarly to
2394                                        Definition 2.1, we define the set of safe data distributions
       safeRLHF R, ϵ, L, λ, DKL (·||πref ) for RLHF as all D ∈ ∆(S×A) such that for all reward func-
2395
       tions R̂ : S×A → R and policies π̂ : S → ∆(A) that satisfy the following two properties:
2396
2397
             1. Low expected error: R̂ is similar to R in expected choice probabilities under D, i.e.:
2398
2399                     E(s,a1 ,a2 )∼D DKL pR (·|s, a1 , a2 )||pR̂ (·|s, a2 , a2 ) ≤ ϵ · range R.
2400         2. Optimality: π̂ is optimal with respect to R̂, i.e.:
2401
                                    π̂ ∈ arg max JR̂ (π) − λ · DKL (π(a|s)||πref (a|s)) .
2402                                          π
2403   we can guarantee that π̂ has regret smaller than L, i.e.:
2404
2405
             3. Low regret: π̂ has a regret smaller than L with respect to R, i.e., RegR (π̂) < L.
2406   Similarly, we define the set  of unsafe data distributions to be the complement of
2407
       safeRLHF R, ϵ, L, λ, DKL (·||πref ) :
2408                                           n                                                      o
       unsafeRLHF R, ϵ, L, λ, DKL (·||πref ) := D ∈ ∆(S×A) | D ∈/ safeRLHF R, ϵ, L, λ, DKL (·||πref ) .

2409
2410
2411
       Note: Property 1 of Definition      C.30 is commonly phrased    as minimizing (with respect to R̂)
2412                              h                               i
2413   the loss −E(s,a1 ,a2 )∼D,pR log(σ(R̂(s, a1 ) − R̂(s, a2 ))) (which includes pR , the probability that
2414   a1 is the preferred action over a2 , in the expectation). Our version of Property 1 is equiv-
2415   alent to this and can be derived from the former by adding the constant (w.r.t. R̂) term
2416   E(s,a1 ,a2 )∼D,pR [log(σ(R(s, a1 ) − R(s, a2 )))].
2417

2912   C.5   A REGULARIZED NEGATIVE RESULT FOR GENERAL MDP S
2913
2914   Throughout, let ⟨S, A, τ, µ0 , R, γ⟩ be an MDP. Additionally, assume there to be a data distribution
2915   D ∈ ∆(S×A) used for learning the reward function. We do a priori not assume that D is induced by
       a reference policy, but we will specialize to that case later on.


                                                             54
       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


2916
       We also throughout fix ϵ > 0, λ > 0, L ∈ (0, 1), which will represent, respectively, an approximation-
2917   error for the reward function, the regularization strength, and a lower regret bound. Furthermore,
2918   let ω : Π → R be any continuous regularization function of policies with ω(π) ≥ 0 for all
2919   π ∈ Π. For example, if there is a nowhere-zero reference policy πref , then ω could be given by
2920   ω(π) = DKL (π||πref ). For any reward function R̂, a policy π̂ exists that is optimal with respect to
2921   regularized maximization of reward:
2922                                      π̂ ∈ arg max JR̂ (π) − λω(π).
2923                                                                   π
2924   We will try to answer the following question: Do there exist realistic conditions on ω and D for
2925   which there exists R̂ together with π̂ such that the following properties hold?
2926                      h               i

                                range R      ≤ ϵ.

2932   to be deterministic.

       dividing by range R, we obtain the result.

       We now present another specialization of Theorem C.41. Namely, from now on, assume that
3187   D = Dπref and ω(π) = DKL (π||πref ). In other words, the dataset used to evaluate the reward
3188   function is sampled from the same (safe) policy used in KL-regularization. This leads to the following
3189   condition specializing the one from Equation (108):
3190
                                                                    ϵ
3191                             Dπref (supp Dπ∗ ) ≤            λ·DKL (π∗ ||πref )
                                                                                    .                    (109)
3192                                                   1 + range R·C(δ,π∗ ,µ0 ,τ,γ)
3193
       πref now appears on both the left and right side of the equation, and so one can wonder whether
3194   it is ever possible that the inequality holds. After all, if Dπref (supp Dπ∗ ) “gets smaller”, then
3195   DKL (π∗ ||πref ) should usually get “larger”. However, halfing each of the probabilities Dπref (s, a)
3196   for (s, a) ∈ supp Dπ∗ leads to only an increase by the addition of log 2 of DKL (π∗ ||πref ). Thus,
3197   intuitively, we expect the inequality to hold when the left-hand-side is very small. An issue is that the
3198   KL divergence can disproportionately blow up in size if some individual probabilities Dπref (s, a) for
3199   (s, a) ∈ supp Dπ∗ are very small compared to other such probabilities. This can be avoided by a
3200   bound in the proportional difference of these probabilities. We thus obtain the following sufficient
3201   condition for a “negative result”:5

       D     R EQUIREMENTS FOR SAFE OPTIMIZATION
3262
       In this section, we answer the question under which circumstances we can guarantee a safe optimiza-
3263
       tion of a given reward function. Wherever applicable, we make the same assumptions as stated in
3264
       Appendix C.1.
3265
3266
3267
       D.1   A PPLYING B ERGE ’ S MAXIMUM THEOREM
3268
       Definition D.1 (Correspondence). Let X, Y be two sets. A correspondence C : X ⇒ Y is a function
3269   X → P(Y ) from X to the power set of Y .
3270
3271
       Definition D.2 (Upper Hemicontinuous, Lower Hemicontinuous, Continuous, Compact-Valued). Let
       C : X ⇒ Y be a correspondence where X and Y are topological spaces. Then:
3272
3273
              • C is called upper hemicontinuous if for every x ∈ X and every open set V ⊆ Y with
3274            C(x) ⊆ V , there exists an open set U ⊆ X with x ∈ U and such that for all x′ ∈ U one
3275            has C(x′ ) ⊆ V .
3276
3277          • C is called lower hemicontinuous if for every x ∈ X and every open set V ⊆ Y with
3278            C(x) ∩ V ̸= ∅, there exists an open set U ⊆ X with x ∈ U and such that for all x′ ∈ U
3279            one has C(x′ ) ∩ V ̸= ∅.
3280          • C is called continuous if it is both upper and lower hemicontinuous.
3281
3282          • C is called compact-valued if C(x) is a compact subset of Y for all x ∈ X.
3283   Theorem D.3 (Maximum Theorem, (Berge, 1963)). Let Θ and X be topological spaces, f : Θ×X →
3284   R a continuous function, and C : Θ ⇒ X be a continuous, compact-valued correspondence such
3285   that C(θ) ̸= ∅ for all θ ∈ Θ. Define the optimal value function f ∗ : Θ → R by
3286
3287                                           f ∗ (θ) := max f (θ, x)
                                                        x∈C(θ)
3288
3289   and the maximizer function C ∗ : Θ ⇒ X by
3290
                           C ∗ (θ) := arg max f (θ, x) = x ∈ C(θ) | f (θ, x) = f ∗ (θ) .

3291                                  x∈C(θ)
3292
3293   Then f ∗ is continuous and C ∗ is a compact-valued, upper hemicontinuous correspondence with
       nonempty values, i.e. C ∗ (θ) ̸= ∅ for all θ ∈ Θ.


                                                         61
       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


3294
       We now show that this theorem corresponds to our setting. Namely, replace   X be by Π, the set of all
3295   policies. Every policy π ∈ Π can be viewed as a vector ⃗π = π(a | s) s∈S,a∈A ∈ RS×A , and so we

3296
       view Π as a subset of RS×A . Π inherits the standard Euclidean metric and thus topology from RS×A .
3297
       Replace Θ by R, the  set of all reward functions. We can view each reward function R ∈ R as a
3298          ⃗ = R(s, a)
       vector R              (s,a)∈S×A
                                        ∈ RS×A . So we view R as a subset of RS×A and thus a topological
3299
3300   space. Replace f by the function J : R × Π → R given by
3301                                                                              ⃗
                                                       J(R, π) := J R (π) = η π · R.
3302
3303   Take as the correspondence C : R ⇒ Π the trivial function C(R) := Π that maps every reward
3304
       function to the full set of policies.
3305   Proposition D.4. These definitions satisfy the conditions of Theorem D.3, that is:
3306
3307
              1. J : R × Π → R is continuous.
3308          2. C : R ⇒ Π is continuous and compact-valued with non-empty values.
3309
3310   Proof. Let us prove 1. Since the scalar product is continuous, it is enough to show that η : Π → RS×A
3311   is continuous. Let (s, a) ∈ S×A be arbitrary. Then it is enough to show that each componentfunction
3312   η(s, a) : Π → R given by
                                                η(s, a) (π) := η π (s, a)

3313

                               ∗

       empty values.


                                                                         62
       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


3348
       Proof. This follows from Theorem D.3 and Proposition D.4.
3349
3350
       In particular, every reward function has a compact and non-empty set of optimal policies, and their
3351   value changes continuously with the reward function. The most important part of the corollary is the
3352   upper hemicontinuity, which has the following consequence:
3353
       Corollary D.6. Let R be a fixed, non-trivial reward function, meaning that max J R ̸= min J R . Let
3354
       U ∈ (0, 1] be arbitrary. Then there exists ϵ > 0 such that for all R̂ ∈ Bϵ (R) and all π̂ ∈ Π∗ (R̂), we
3355
       have RegR (π̂) < U .
3356
3357
3358
       Proof. The condition max J R ̸= min J R ensures that the regret function RegR : Π → [0, 1] is
       well-defined. Recall its definition:
3359
3360                                                    max J R − J R (π)
                                         RegR (π) =                       .
3361                                                    max J R − min J R
3362
       Since J R is continuous by Proposition D.4, the regret function RegR is continuous as well. Conse-
3363                                −1
       quently, the set V := RegR

                                       [0, U ) is open in Π.
3364
3365   Notice that Π∗ (R) ⊆ V (optimal policies have no regret). Thus, by Corollary D.5, there exists an
3366   open set W ⊆ R with R ∈ W such that for all R̂ ∈ W we have Π∗ (R̂) ⊆ V . Consequently, for
3367   all π̂ ∈ Π∗ (R̂), we get RegR (π̂) < U . Since W is open, it contains a whole epsilon ball around R,
3368   showing the result.
3369
3370   Now we translate the results to the distance defined by D, a data distribution. Namely, let D ∈
3371   ∆(S×A) a distribution that assigns a positive probability to each transition. Then define the D-norm
3372   by
                                       dD (R) := E(s,a)∼D R(s, a) .

3373
3374   This is indeed a norm, i.e.: for all α ∈ R and all R, R′ ∈ R, we have
3375
3376          • dD (R + R′ ) ≤ dD (R) + dD (R′ );
3377          • dD (α · R) = |α| · dD (R);
3378
              • dD (R) = 0 if and only if R = 0.
3379
3380   For the third property, one needs the assumption that D(s, a) > 0 for all (s, a) ∈ S×A.
3381
3382
       This norm then induces a metric that we denote the same way:
3383                                        dD (R, R′ ) := dD (R − R′ ).
3384
       We obtain:
3385

3419
       Remark D.8. If c := min(s,a)∈S×A D(s, a) is very small, then the proof of the preceding corollary
3420   shows that dD (R, R̂) must be correspondingly smaller to guarantee a low regret of π̂ ∈ Π∗ (R̂). This
3421   makes sense since a large effective distance between R and R̂ can “hide” in the regions where D is
3422   small when distance is measured via dD .

       D.3    S AFE OPTIMIZATION VIA APPROXIMATED CHOICE PROBABILITIES

3605   In this section, we will show that for any chosen upper regret bound U , there is an ϵ > 0 s.t. if the

3713   Lemma D.18. For q ∈ (0, 1), define fq : (0, 1) → R by

3714                                fq (p) := − q log p + (1 − q) log(1 − p) .
3715   Then for all δ > 0 there exists µ(δ) > 0 such that for all p ∈ (0, 1) with fq (p) < H(q) + µ(δ), we
3716   have |p − q| < δ. Concretely, one can choose µ(δ) := 2δ 2 .
3717
3718   Proof. Let δ > 0 and define µ(δ) := 2δ 2 . Assume that fq (p) < H(q) + µ(δ). By Pinker’s inequality,
3719   we have
3720                                                  q                 1−q
                                   2(p − q)2 ≤ q log + (1 − q) · log
3721                                                  p                 1−p
3722                                         = −H(q) + fq (p)
3723
                                             < µ(δ)
3724
3725                                           = 2δ 2 .
       Consequently, we have |p − q| < δ.

3788   D.4    P OSITIVE RESULT FOR REGULARIZED RLHF
3789
3790   Here, we present simple positive results for regularized RLHF, both in a version with the expected
3791   reward distance, and in a version using the distance in choice probabilities. Some of it will directly
3792   draw from the positive results proved before.
3793   Theorem D.21. Let λ ∈ (0, ∞) be given and fixed. Assume we are given an MDP ⟨S, A, τ, µ0 , R, γ⟩,
3794   and a data distribution D ∈ S×A which assigns positive probability to all transitions, i.e., ∀(s, a) ∈
3795   S×A, D(s, a) > 0. Let ω : Π → R be a continuous regularization function that has a reference
3796   policy πref as one of its minima.6 Assume that πref is not (λ, ω)-optimal for R and let L =
3797   RegR (πref ). Then there exists ϵ > 0 such that D ∈ safe(R, ϵ, L, λ, ω).
3798
3799   Proof. We prove the theorem by showing that for every D ∈ ∆(S×A) hsuch that D(s, a)
                                                                                        i > 0 for
                                                                                |R̂(s,a)−R(s,a)|
3800   all (s, a) ∈ S×A, there exists ϵ > 0 such that for all R̂ with E(s,a)∼D       range R     < ϵ and
3801                                                                    R               R
       all policies π̂ that are (λ, ω)-RLHF optimal wrt. R̂, we have Reg (π̂) < Reg (πref ). Because
3802
       L = RegR (π̂) < RegR (πref ) this proves that then D ∈ safe(R, ϵ, L, λ, ω).
3803
3804   The proof is an application of Berge’s maximum Theorem, Theorem D.3. Namely, define the function
3805
                                  f : R × Π → R,        f (R, π) := JR (π) − λω(π).
3806
3807   Furthermore, define the correspondence C : R ⇒ Π as the trivial map C(R) = Π. Let f ∗ : R → R
                                                                                  ∗    := maxπ∈Π f (R, π).
3808   map a reward function to the value of a (λ, ω)-RLHF optimal
                                                                   policy, i.e., f (R)
3809   Define C as the corresponding argmax, i.e., C (R) := π | f (R, π) = f ∗ (R) . Assume on R
                 ∗                                       ∗

3810   we have the standard Euclidean topology. Since ω is assumed continuous and by Proposition D.4
3811
       also J is continuous, it follows that f is continuous. Thus, Theorem D.3 implies that C ∗ is upper
       hemicontinuous, see Definition D.2. The rest of the proof is simply an elaboration of why upper
3812
       hemicontinuity of C ∗ gives the result.
3813
3814   Now, define the set
                                    V := π ′ ∈ Π | RegR (π ′ ) < RegR (πref ) .

3815
3816   Since the regret is a continuous function, this set is open. Now, let π ∈ C ∗ (R) be (λ, ω)-RLHF
3817   optimal with respect to R. It follows
3818
                                            JR (π) = f (R, π) + λω(π)
3819
3820
                                                   > f (R, πref ) + λω(πref )
3821                                               = JR (πref ),
3822   where we used the optimality of π for f , that πref is not optimal for it, and that πref is the minimum
3823   of ω. So overall, this shows C ∗ (R) ⊆ V.
3824

                                         ∗

                        |R̂(s,a)−R(s,a)|

3828   topology. Then for all such R̂ and any policy π̂ that is (λ, ω)-RLHF optimal wrt. R̂, we by definition
3829   have
3830                                              π̂ ∈ C ∗ (R̂) ⊆ V,

       Now, we show the same result, but with the choice distance instead of expected reward distance:

       Theorem D.22. Let λ ∈ (0, ∞) be given and fixed. Assume we are given an MDP ⟨S, A, τ, µ0 , R, γ⟩,
       and a data distribution D ∈ S×A which assigns positive probability to all transitions, i.e., ∀(s, a) ∈
3837
       S×A, D(s, a) > 0. Let ω : Π → R be a continuous regularization function that has a reference
3838
       policy πref as one of its minima. Assume that πref is not (λ, ω)-optimal for R and let L = RegR (πref ).
3839                                                DKL

3840
       Then there exists ϵ > 0 such that D ∈ safe        R, ϵ, L, λ, ω .
3841
3842   Proof. Let G := RΞ be the vector space of return functions, which becomes a topological space when
3843
       equipped with the infinity norm. Define the function
3844                              f : G × Π → R,       f (G, π) := J G (π) − λω(π),
3845
3846   where J G (π) := Eξ∼π [G(ξ)] is the policy evaluation function of the return function G. f is
3847   continuous. Define the correspondence C : G ⇒ Π as the trivial map C(G) = Π. Let f ∗ : G → R
3848   map a return function to the value of a (λ, ω)-optimal policy, i.e., f ∗ (G) := maxπ∈Π f (G, π). Define
3849
       C ∗ as the corresponding argmax. Then Theorem D.3 implies that C ∗ is upper hemicontinuous, see
       Definition D.2. As in the previous proof, the rest is an elaboration of why this gives the desired result.
3850
3851   Set G as the return function corresponding to R. Define
3852
                                        V := π ′ ∈ Π | RegR (π ′ ) < L .

3853
3854
3855   We now claim that C ∗ (G) ⊆ V. Indeed, let π ∈ C ∗ (G). Then
3856                                      J R (π) = f (G, π) + λω(π)
3857
                                                  > f (G, πref ) + λω(πref )
3858
3859                                              = J R (πref ).
3860   Note that we used the optimality of π for f , that πref is not optimal for it, and also that πref minimizes
3861
       ω by assumption. This shows RegR (π) < RegR (πref ) = L, and thus the claim.
3862
3863   Since C ∗ is upper hemicontinuous and V an open set, this implies that there exists σ > 0 such that
3864   for all Ĝ ∈ G with G − Ĝ ∞ < σ, we have C ∗ (Ĝ) ⊆ V.
3865
       Now, define ϵ := ϵ(σ) as in Corollary D.20 and let R̂ be any reward function with dD
                                                                                          KL (R, R̂) < ϵ.
3866
3867
       Then by that corollary, there exists c ∈ R such that G − Ĝ − c ∞ < σ. Consequently, we have
3868   C ∗ (Ĝ) = C ∗ (Ĝ − c) ⊆ V by what we showed before, which shows the result.
